religion">a series</a> on</td></tr><tr><th class="sidebar-title-with-pretitle" style="background: #FF0000;"><span style="font-size:110%"><a class="mw-selflink selflink"><span style="color:#FFFFFF;">Chinese folk religion</span></a></span></th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E7%A6%84_l%C3%B9_or_%E5%AD%90_zi_symbol---red.svg" title='Stylisation of the 禄 lù or 子 zi grapheme, respectively meaning "prosperity", "furthering", "welfare" and "son", "offspring". 字 zì, meaning "word" and "symbol", is a cognate of 子 zi and represents a "son" enshrined under a "roof". The symbol is ultimately a representation of the north celestial pole (Běijí 北极) and its spinning constellations, and as such it is equivalent to the Eurasian symbol of the swastika, 卍 wàn.'><img alt='Stylisation of the 禄 lù or 子 zi grapheme, respectively meaning "prosperity", "furthering", "welfare" and "son", "offspring". 字 zì, meaning "word" and "symbol", is a cognate of 子 zi and represents a "son" enshrined under a "roof". The symbol is ultimately a representation of the north celestial pole (Běijí 北极) and its spinning constellations, and as such it is equivalent to the Eurasian symbol of the swastika, 卍 wàn.' data-file-height="194" data-file-width="194" decoding="async" height="75" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/%E7%A6%84_l%C3%B9_or_%E5%AD%90_zi_symbol---red.svg/75px-%E7%A6%84_l%C3%B9_or_%E5%AD%90_zi_symbol---red.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/%E7%A6%84_l%C3%B9_or_%E5%AD%90_zi_symbol---red.svg/113px-%E7%A6%84_l%C3%B9_or_%E5%AD%90_zi_symbol---red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/%E7%A6%84_l%C3%B9_or_%E5%AD%90_zi_symbol---red.svg/150px-%E7%A6%84_l%C3%B9_or_%E5%AD%90_zi_symbol---red.svg.png 2x" width="75"/></a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E6%96%87%E6%BE%B3_%E5%9F%8E%E9%9A%8D%E5%BB%9F.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2513" data-file-width="3467" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/%E6%96%87%E6%BE%B3_%E5%9F%8E%E9%9A%8D%E5%BB%9F.jpg/220px-%E6%96%87%E6%BE%B3_%E5%9F%8E%E9%9A%8D%E5%BB%9F.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/%E6%96%87%E6%BE%B3_%E5%9F%8E%E9%9A%8D%E5%BB%9F.jpg/330px-%E6%96%87%E6%BE%B3_%E5%9F%8E%E9%9A%8D%E5%BB%9F.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/%E6%96%87%E6%BE%B3_%E5%9F%8E%E9%9A%8D%E5%BB%9F.jpg/440px-%E6%96%87%E6%BE%B3_%E5%9F%8E%E9%9A%8D%E5%BB%9F.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E6%96%87%E6%BE%B3_%E5%9F%8E%E9%9A%8D%E5%BB%9F.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Temple of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chenghuangshen" title="Chenghuangshen">City God</a> of Wenao, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magong_City" title="Magong City">Magong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heshen_temple_in_Hequ,_Xinzhou,_Shanxi,_China.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="3868" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Heshen_temple_in_Hequ%2C_Xinzhou%2C_Shanxi%2C_China.jpg/220px-Heshen_temple_in_Hequ%2C_Xinzhou%2C_Shanxi%2C_China.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Heshen_temple_in_Hequ%2C_Xinzhou%2C_Shanxi%2C_China.jpg/330px-Heshen_temple_in_Hequ%2C_Xinzhou%2C_Shanxi%2C_China.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Heshen_temple_in_Hequ%2C_Xinzhou%2C_Shanxi%2C_China.jpg/440px-Heshen_temple_in_Hequ%2C_Xinzhou%2C_Shanxi%2C_China.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heshen_temple_in_Hequ,_Xinzhou,_Shanxi,_China.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Temple of <a href="/wiki/Hebo" title="Hebo">Hebo</a> ("River Lord"), the god (Heshen, "River god") of the sacred <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Hequ_County" title="Hequ County">Hequ</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xinzhou" title="Xinzhou">Xinzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Five_officials_haikou_2010_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Five_officials_haikou_2010_01.jpg/220px-Five_officials_haikou_2010_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Five_officials_haikou_2010_01.jpg/330px-Five_officials_haikou_2010_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Five_officials_haikou_2010_01.jpg/440px-Five_officials_haikou_2010_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Five_officials_haikou_2010_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Altar to the Five Officials worshipped inside the Temple of the Five Lords in <a href="/wiki/Haikou" title="Haikou">Haikou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hainan" title="Hainan">Hainan</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><b>Chinese folk religion</b>, also known as <b>Chinese popular religion</b> comprehends a range of traditional religious practices of <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a>, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_diaspora" title="Chinese diaspora">Chinese diaspora</a>. Vivienne Wee described it as "an empty bowl, which can variously be filled with the contents of institutionalised religions such as <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Buddhism" title="Chinese Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>, the Chinese syncretic religions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> This includes the veneration of <a href="/wiki/Shen_(Chinese_religion)" title="Shen (Chinese religion)"><i>shen</i> (spirits)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_ancestor_worship" title="Chinese ancestor worship">ancestors</a>, exorcism of demonic forces, and a belief in the rational order of nature, balance in the universe and reality that can be influenced by human beings and their rulers, as well as spirits and gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeiser1995378_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeiser1995378-2">[2]</a></sup> Worship is devoted to <a href="/wiki/Chinese_gods_and_immortals" title="Chinese gods and immortals">gods and immortals</a>, who can be <a href="/wiki/Shen_(Chinese_religion)" title="Shen (Chinese religion)">deities</a> of places or natural phenomena, of human behaviour, or <a href="/wiki/Progenitor" title="Progenitor">founders</a> of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_kin" title="Chinese kin">family lineages</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">Stories</a> of these gods are collected into the body of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_mythology" title="Chinese mythology">Chinese mythology</a>. By the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> (960-1279), these practices had been blended with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> doctrines and <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoist</a> teachings to form the popular religious system which has lasted in many ways until the present day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvermyer198651_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvermyer198651-3">[3]</a></sup> The present day government of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">mainland China</a>, like the imperial dynasties, tolerates popular religious organizations if they bolster social stability but suppresses or persecutes those that they fear would undermine it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the fall of the empire in 1911, governments and modernizing elites condemned "feudal superstition" and opposed or attempted to eradicate traditional religion in order to promote modern values. By the late 20th century, these attitudes began to change both in Taiwan and in mainland China. Many scholars now view folk religion in a positive light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaenssbauer201528-37_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaenssbauer201528-37-5">[5]</a></sup> In recent times traditional religion is experiencing a revival in both China and Taiwan. Some forms have received official understanding or recognition as a preservation of traditional culture, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazu_(goddess)" title="Mazu (goddess)">Mazuism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sanyi_teaching" title="Sanyi teaching">Sanyi teaching</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RCTC2014_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-RCTC2014-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Emperor" title="Yellow Emperor">Huangdi</a> worship,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and other forms of local worship, for example the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Longwang" title="Longwang">Longwang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pangu" title="Pangu">Pangu</a> or <a href="/wiki/Caishen" title="Caishen">Caishen</a> worship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Feng_shui" title="Feng shui">Geomancy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acupuncture" title="Acupuncture">acupuncture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional Chinese medicine</a> reflect this world view, since features of the landscape as well as organs of the body are in correlation with the five powers and yin and yang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvermeyer1986[httpswwwgooglecombookseditionReligions_of_ChinanYkbAAAAQBAJhlengbpv1bsqmedicine_86]_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvermeyer1986[httpswwwgooglecombookseditionReligions_of_ChinanYkbAAAAQBAJhlengbpv1bsqmedicine_86]-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diversity">Diversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Diversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Chinese religions have a variety of sources, local forms, founder backgrounds, and ritual and philosophical traditions. Despite this diversity, there is a common core that can be summarised as four theological, cosmological, and moral concepts:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20135–6_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20135–6-10">[10]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Tian" title="Tian">Tian</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">天</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">tiān</span></i>; <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></small> 'Heaven'), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_(philosophy)" title="Absolute (philosophy)">transcendent source</a> of moral meaning; <i><a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">qi</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">氣</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">qì</span></i>), the breath or energy that animates the universe; <i><a href="/wiki/Ancestor_veneration_in_China" title="Ancestor veneration in China">jingzu</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">敬祖</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">jìng zǔ</span></i>), the veneration of ancestors; and <i><a href="/wiki/Bao_ying" title="Bao ying">bao ying</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">報應</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">bàoyìng</span></i>), moral reciprocity; together with two traditional concepts of fate and meaning:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201321_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201321-11">[11]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Ming_yun" title="Ming yun">ming yun</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">命運</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">mìngyùn</span></i>), the personal destiny or burgeoning; and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuan_fen" title="Yuan fen">yuan fen</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">緣分</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">yuánfèn</span></i>), “fateful <a href="/wiki/Coincidence" title="Coincidence">coincidence</a>”,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201323_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201323-12">[12]</a></sup> good and bad chances and potential relationships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201323_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201323-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Yin_and_yang" title="Yin and yang">Yin and yang</a> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">陰陽</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">yīnyáng</span></i>) is the polarity that describes the order of the universe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdler2011[httpwww2kenyoneduDeptsReligionFacAdlerWritingsNon-theisticpdf_13]_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdler2011[httpwww2kenyoneduDeptsReligionFacAdlerWritingsNon-theisticpdf_13]-13">[13]</a></sup> held in balance by the interaction of principles of “extension” (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">神</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">shén</span></i>; <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></small> 'spirit') and principles of “returning” (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">鬼</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">guǐ</span></i>; <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></small> 'ghost'),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Teiser,_1996_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Teiser,_1996-14">[14]</a></sup>  with <i>yang</i> ("act") usually preferred over <i>yin</i> ("receptiveness") in common religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thien_Do,_2003,_pp._10-11_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thien_Do,_2003,_pp._10-11-15">[15]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Ling_(Chinese_religion)" title="Ling (Chinese religion)">Ling</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">靈</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">líng</span></i>), "<a href="/wiki/Numen" title="Numen">numen</a>" or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacred" title="Sacred">sacred</a>", is the "medium" of the two states and the inchoate order of creation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thien_Do,_2003,_pp._10-11_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thien_Do,_2003,_pp._10-11-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dongmen_City_God_Temple,_2014-10-07_17.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="5472" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Dongmen_City_God_Temple%2C_2014-10-07_17.JPG/220px-Dongmen_City_God_Temple%2C_2014-10-07_17.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Dongmen_City_God_Temple%2C_2014-10-07_17.JPG/330px-Dongmen_City_God_Temple%2C_2014-10-07_17.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Dongmen_City_God_Temple%2C_2014-10-07_17.JPG/440px-Dongmen_City_God_Temple%2C_2014-10-07_17.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dongmen_City_God_Temple,_2014-10-07_17.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Temple of the City God of Dongmen, in <a href="/wiki/Xiangshan_County,_Zhejiang" title="Xiangshan County, Zhejiang">Xiangshan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ningbo" title="Ningbo">Ningbo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E6%B5%9E%E6%99%AF%E5%AF%BA_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2537" data-file-width="3609" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/%E6%B5%9E%E6%99%AF%E5%AF%BA_01.jpg/220px-%E6%B5%9E%E6%99%AF%E5%AF%BA_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/%E6%B5%9E%E6%99%AF%E5%AF%BA_01.jpg/330px-%E6%B5%9E%E6%99%AF%E5%AF%BA_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/%E6%B5%9E%E6%99%AF%E5%AF%BA_01.jpg/440px-%E6%B5%9E%E6%99%AF%E5%AF%BA_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E6%B5%9E%E6%99%AF%E5%AF%BA_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sign reading "This is a place of folk belief. No religious donation or religious activities are allowed." Taken in a Chinese folk temple in <a href="/wiki/Weifang" title="Weifang">Weifang City</a>, Shandong Province</div></div></div>
<p>The Chinese language historically has not had a concept or overarching name for "religion". In English, the terms "popular religion" or  "folk religion" have long been used to mean local religious life. In Chinese academic literature and common usage "folk religion" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">民間宗教</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">mínjiān zōngjiào</span></i>) refers to specific <a href="/wiki/Chinese_salvationist_religions" title="Chinese salvationist religions">organised folk religious sects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Contemporary academic study of traditional cults and the creation of a government agency that gave legal status to this religion <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClart2014399–401_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClart2014399–401-17">[17]</a></sup> have created proposals to formalise names and deal more clearly with folk religious sects and help conceptualise research and administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClart2014402_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClart2014402-18">[18]</a></sup> The terms that have been proposed include "Chinese native religion" or "Chinese indigenous religion" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">民俗宗教</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">mínsú zōngjiào</span></i>), "Chinese ethnic religion" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">民族宗教</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">mínzú zōngjiào</span></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClart2014402–406_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClart2014402–406-19">[19]</a></sup> or simply "Chinese religion" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">中華教</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">zhōnghuájiào</span></i>) viewed as comparable to the usage of the term "<a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>" for Indian religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClart2014409_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClart2014409-20">[20]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, reports the scholar Tan Chee-Beng, Chinese do not have a definite term for their traditional religion, which is not surprising because "the religion is diffused into various aspects of Chinese culture". They refer to their religion as <i>bai fo</i> or <i>bai shen</i>, which prompted Allan A. J. Elliott to suggest the term "<i>shenism</i>" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">神教</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">shénjiào</span></i>).  Tan however, comments that is not the way the Chinese refer to their religion, which in any case includes worship of ancestors, not simply <i>shen</i>, and suggests it is logical to simply use "Chinese Religion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETan1983219_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETan1983219-21">[21]</a></sup> "Shenxianism" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">神仙教</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">shénxiān jiào</span></i>, literally, "religion of <a href="/wiki/Shen_(Chinese_religion)" title="Shen (Chinese religion)">deities</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xian_(Taoism)" title="Xian (Taoism)">immortals</a>"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShi2008_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShi2008-22">[22]</a></sup> is a term partly inspired by Elliott's neologism, "Shenism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClart2014409,_note_35_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClart2014409,_note_35-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a> scholars Yao Wendong and Chen Jialin used the term <i>shenjiao</i> not referring to <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a> as a definite religious system, but to local <i><a href="/wiki/Kami" title="Kami">shin</a></i> beliefs in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Other terms are "folk cults" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">民間崇拜</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">mínjiān chóngbài</span></i>), "spontaneous religion" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">自發宗教</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">zìfā zōngjiào</span></i>), "lived (or living) religion" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">生活宗教</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">shēnghuó zōngjiào</span></i>), "local religion" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">地方宗教</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">dìfāng zōngjiào</span></i>), and "diffused religion" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">分散性宗教</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">fēnsàn xìng zōngjiào</span></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShi2008158–159_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShi2008158–159-25">[25]</a></sup> "Folk beliefs" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">民間信仰</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">mínjiān xìnyǎng</span></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClart2014397_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClart2014397-26">[26]</a></sup> is a seldom used term taken by scholars in colonial Taiwan from Japanese during <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_under_Japanese_rule" title="Taiwan under Japanese rule">Japan's occupation</a> (1895–1945). It was used between the 1990s and the early 21st century among mainland Chinese scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang20113_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang20113-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>"Shendao" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">神道</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">shéndào</span></i>; <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></small> 'the Way of the Gods') is a term already used in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yijing" title="Yijing">Yijing</a></i> referring to the divine order of nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Around the time of the spread of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han period</a> (206 BCE – 220 CE), it was used to distinguish the indigenous ancient religion from the imported religion. <a href="/wiki/Ge_Hong" title="Ge Hong">Ge Hong</a> used it in his <i><a href="/wiki/Baopuzi" title="Baopuzi">Baopuzi</a></i> as a synonym for <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The term was subsequently adopted in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> in the 6th century as <i>Shindo</i>, later <i>Shinto</i>, with the same purpose of identification of the Japanese indigenous religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In the 14th century, the <a href="/wiki/Hongwu_Emperor" title="Hongwu Emperor">Hongwu Emperor</a> (Taizu of the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a>, 1328–1398) used the term "Shendao" clearly identifying the indigenous cults, which he strengthened and systematised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>"Chinese Universism", not in the sense of "<a href="/wiki/Universalism" title="Universalism">universalism</a>", that is a system of universal application, that is <a href="/wiki/Tian" title="Tian">Tian</a> in Chinese thought, is a coinage of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jan_Jakob_Maria_de_Groot" title="Jan Jakob Maria de Groot">Jan Jakob Maria de Groot</a> that refers to the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">metaphysical</a> perspective that lies behind the Chinese religious tradition. De Groot calls Chinese Universism "the ancient metaphysical view that serves as the basis of all classical Chinese thought. ... In Universism, the three components of integrated universe—understood epistemologically, 'heaven, earth and man', and understood ontologically, '<a href="/wiki/Taiji_(philosophy)" title="Taiji (philosophy)">Taiji</a> (the great beginning, the highest ultimate), yin and yang'—are formed".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1931 <a href="/wiki/Hu_Shih" title="Hu Shih">Hu Shih</a> argued that "Two great religions have played tremendously important roles throughout Chinese history. One is Buddhism which came to China probably before the Christian era but which began to exert nation-wide influence only after the third century A.D. The other great religion has had no generic name, but I propose to call it Siniticism. It is the native ancient religion of the <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> people: it dates back to time immemorial, over 10,000 years old, and includes all such later phases of its development as Moism, Confucianism (as a state religion), and all the various stages of the Taoist religion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attributes">Attributes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Attributes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Contemporary Chinese scholars have identified what they find to be the essential features of the ancient (or indigenous—ethnic) religion of China. According to Chen Xiaoyi (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">陳曉毅</span>) local <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_religion" title="Indigenous religion">indigenous religion</a> is the crucial factor for a harmonious "religious ecology" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">宗教生態</span>), that is the balance of forces in a given community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClart2014405_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClart2014405-35">[35]</a></sup> Professor Han Bingfang (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">韓秉芳</span>) has called for a <a href="/wiki/Rectification_of_names" title="Rectification of names">rectification of distorted names</a> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">正名</span>). Distorted names are "superstitious activities" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">迷信活動</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">míxìn huódòng</span></i>) or "feudal superstition" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">封建迷信</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">fēngjiàn míxìn</span></i>), that were derogatorily applied to the indigenous religion by leftist policies. Christian missionaries also used the propaganda label "feudal superstition" in order to undermine their religious competitor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClart2014408_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClart2014408-36">[36]</a></sup> Han calls for the acknowledgment of the ancient Chinese religion for what it really is, the "core and soul of popular culture" (<a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">sú wénhuà de héxīn yǔ línghún</span></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClart2014407_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClart2014407-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Chen Jinguo (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">陳進國</span>), the ancient Chinese religion is a core element of Chinese cultural and religious self-awareness (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">文化自覺, 信仰自覺</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">wénhuà zìjué, xìnyǎng zìjué</span></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClart2014408_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClart2014408-36">[36]</a></sup> He has proposed a theoretical definition of Chinese indigenous religion in "three inseparable attributes" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">三位一體</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">sānwèiyītǐ</span></i>), apparently inspired to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Junyi" title="Tang Junyi">Tang Junyi</a>'s thought:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClart2014408–409_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClart2014408–409-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>substance (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">體</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">tǐ</span></i>): religiousness (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">宗教性</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">zōngjiào xìng</span></i>);</li>
<li>function (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">用</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">yòng</span></i>): folkloricity (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">民俗性</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">mínsú xìng</span></i>);</li>
<li>quality (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">相</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">xiàng</span></i>): Chineseness (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">中華性</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">zhōnghuá xìng</span></i>).</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_creation_myth" title="Chinese creation myth">Chinese creation myth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_spiritual_world_concepts" title="Chinese spiritual world concepts">Chinese spiritual world concepts</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Worship_at_the_Great_Temple_of_Shennong-Yandi_in_Suizhou,_Hubei.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Worship_at_the_Great_Temple_of_Shennong-Yandi_in_Suizhou%2C_Hubei.jpg/220px-Worship_at_the_Great_Temple_of_Shennong-Yandi_in_Suizhou%2C_Hubei.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Worship_at_the_Great_Temple_of_Shennong-Yandi_in_Suizhou%2C_Hubei.jpg/330px-Worship_at_the_Great_Temple_of_Shennong-Yandi_in_Suizhou%2C_Hubei.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Worship_at_the_Great_Temple_of_Shennong-Yandi_in_Suizhou%2C_Hubei.jpg/440px-Worship_at_the_Great_Temple_of_Shennong-Yandi_in_Suizhou%2C_Hubei.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Worship_at_the_Great_Temple_of_Shennong-Yandi_in_Suizhou,_Hubei.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Communal ceremony at the Great Temple of <a href="/wiki/Yan_Emperor" title="Yan Emperor">Yandi</a> <a href="/wiki/Shennong" title="Shennong">Shennong</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">炎帝神農大殿</span> <i>Yándì Shénnóng dàdiàn</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Suizhou" title="Suizhou">Suizhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hubei" title="Hubei">Hubei</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E5%AA%BD%E7%A5%96%E5%A8%98%E5%A8%98002.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2376" decoding="async" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/%E5%AA%BD%E7%A5%96%E5%A8%98%E5%A8%98002.JPG/170px-%E5%AA%BD%E7%A5%96%E5%A8%98%E5%A8%98002.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/%E5%AA%BD%E7%A5%96%E5%A8%98%E5%A8%98002.JPG/255px-%E5%AA%BD%E7%A5%96%E5%A8%98%E5%A8%98002.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/%E5%AA%BD%E7%A5%96%E5%A8%98%E5%A8%98002.JPG/340px-%E5%AA%BD%E7%A5%96%E5%A8%98%E5%A8%98002.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E5%AA%BD%E7%A5%96%E5%A8%98%E5%A8%98002.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazu_(goddess)" title="Mazu (goddess)">Mazu</a> at a temple in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chiayi_City" title="Chiayi City">Chiayi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diversity_and_unity">Diversity and unity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Diversity and unity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ancient Chinese religious practices are diverse, varying from province to province and even from one village to another, for religious behaviour is bound to local communities, kinship, and environments. In each setting, institution and ritual behaviour assumes highly organised forms. Temples and the gods in them acquire symbolic character and perform specific functions involved in the everyday life of the local community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20135_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20135-39">[39]</a></sup> Local religion preserves aspects of natural beliefs such as <a href="/wiki/Totem" title="Totem">totemism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang,_2004._pp._60-61_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang,_2004._pp._60-61-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">shamanism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ancient Chinese religion pervades all aspects of social life. Many scholars, following the lead of sociologist <a href="/wiki/C._K._Yang_(sociologist)" title="C. K. Yang (sociologist)">C. K. Yang</a>, see the ancient Chinese religion deeply embedded in family and civic life, rather than expressed in a separate organisational structure like a "church", as in the West.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20134_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20134-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Deity or temple associations and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_lineage_associations" title="Chinese lineage associations">lineage associations</a>, pilgrimage associations and formalised prayers, rituals and expressions of virtues, are the common forms of organisation of Chinese religion on the local level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20135_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20135-39">[39]</a></sup> Neither initiation rituals nor official membership into a church organisation separate from one person's native identity are mandatory in order to be involved in religious activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20135_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20135-39">[39]</a></sup> Contrary to institutional religions, Chinese religion does not require "conversion" for participation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20134_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20134-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>The prime criterion for participation in the ancient Chinese religion is not "to believe" in an official doctrine or <a href="/wiki/Dogma" title="Dogma">dogma</a>, but "to belong" to the local unit of an ancient Chinese religion, that is the "association", the "village" or the "kinship", with their gods and rituals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20135_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20135-39">[39]</a></sup> Scholar Richard Madsen describes the ancient Chinese religion, adopting the definition of <a href="/wiki/Tu_Weiming" title="Tu Weiming">Tu Weiming</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> as characterised by "immanent transcendence" grounded in a devotion to "concrete humanity", focused on building moral community within concrete humanity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madsen,_2013_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Madsen,_2013-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Inextricably linked to the aforementioned question to find an appropriate "name" for the ancient  Chinese religion, is the difficulty to define it or clearly outline its boundaries. Old <a href="/wiki/Sinology" title="Sinology">sinology</a>, especially Western, tried to distinguish "popular" and "élite" traditions (the latter being Confucianism and Taoism conceived as independent systems). Chinese sinology later adopted another dichotomy which continues in contemporary studies, distinguishing "folk beliefs" (<i>minjian xinyang</i>) and "folk religion" (<i>minjian zongjiao</i>), the latter referring to the doctrinal sects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYangHu2012507_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYangHu2012507-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many studies have pointed out that it is impossible to draw clear distinctions, and since the 1970s several sinologists<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (April 2020)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> swung to the idea of a unified "ancient Chinese religion" that would define the Chinese national identity, similarly to Hindu Dharma for <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and Shinto for <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>. Other sinologists who have not espoused the idea of a unified "national religion" have studied Chinese religion as a system of meaning, or have brought further development in C. K. Yang's distinction between "institutional religion" and "diffused religion", the former functioning as a separate body from other social institutions, and the latter intimately part of secular social institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYangHu2012507–508_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYangHu2012507–508-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Imperial_China">Imperial China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Imperial China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>, the ancient Chinese religion mostly consisted of people organising into <i>shè</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">社</span> ("group", "body", local community altars) who worshipped their godly principle. In many cases the "lord of the <i>she</i>" was the god of the earth, and in others a deified virtuous person (<i><a href="/wiki/Xian_(Taoism)" title="Xian (Taoism)">xiān</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">仙</span>, "immortal"). Some cults such as that of Liu Zhang, a king in what is today <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">Shandong</a>, date back to this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200936-37_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200936-37-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the 3rd century on by the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Wei" title="Northern Wei">Northern Wei</a>, accompanying the spread of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> in China, strong influences from the Indian subcontinent penetrated the ancient Chinese indigenous religion. A cult of <a href="/wiki/Ganesha" title="Ganesha">Ganesha</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">象頭神</span> <i>Xiàngtóushén</i>, "Elephant-Head God") is attested in the year 531.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Pollination from <a href="/wiki/Indian_religions" title="Indian religions">Indian religions</a> included processions of carts with images of gods or floats borne on shoulders, with musicians and chanting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200936-37_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200936-37-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="19th.E2.80.9320th_century"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="19th–20th_century">19th–20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 19th–20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quanshan_Tudi_Gong_Gong_-_statues_-_DSCF8317.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Quanshan_Tudi_Gong_Gong_-_statues_-_DSCF8317.JPG/220px-Quanshan_Tudi_Gong_Gong_-_statues_-_DSCF8317.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Quanshan_Tudi_Gong_Gong_-_statues_-_DSCF8317.JPG/330px-Quanshan_Tudi_Gong_Gong_-_statues_-_DSCF8317.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Quanshan_Tudi_Gong_Gong_-_statues_-_DSCF8317.JPG/440px-Quanshan_Tudi_Gong_Gong_-_statues_-_DSCF8317.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Quanshan_Tudi_Gong_Gong_-_statues_-_DSCF8317.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Zitong altar in a temple of <a href="/wiki/Quanzhou" title="Quanzhou">Quanzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a>. To his left there is a statue of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kuixing" title="Kuixing">Kuixing</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The ancient Chinese religion was subject to persecution in the 19th and 20th centuries. Many ancient temples were destroyed during the <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Rebellion" title="Taiping Rebellion">Taiping Rebellion</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Boxer_Rebellion" title="Boxer Rebellion">Boxer Rebellion</a> in the late 1800s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20139_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20139-49">[49]</a></sup> After the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xinhai_Revolution" title="Xinhai Revolution">Xinhai Revolution</a> of 1911 "most temples were turned to other uses or were destroyed, with a few changed into schools".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200943_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200943-50">[50]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War" title="Second Sino-Japanese War">Japanese invasion of China between 1937 and 1945</a> many temples were used as barracks by soldiers and destroyed in warfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20139_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20139-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200945_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200945-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the past, popular cults were regulated by imperial government policies, promoting certain deities while suppressing others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200946_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200946-52">[52]</a></sup> In the 20th century, with the decline of the empire, increasing urbanisation and Western influence, the issue for the new intellectuals who looked to the West was no longer controlling unauthorised worship of unregistered gods, but the delegitimisation of the ancient Chinese religion as a superstitious obstacle to modernisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200950_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200950-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1904, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Policies" title="New Policies">reform policy</a> of the late <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a> provided that schools would be built through the confiscation of temple property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200950_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200950-53">[53]</a></sup> "Anti-superstition" campaigns followed. The <a href="/wiki/Kuomintang" title="Kuomintang">Nationalist</a> government of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%931949)" title="Republic of China (1912–1949)">Republic of China</a> intensified the suppression of the ancient Chinese religion with the 1928 "Standards for retaining or abolishing gods and shrines"; the policy attempted to abolish the cults of all gods with the exception of ancient great human heroes and sages such as Yu the Great, <a href="/wiki/Guan_Yu" title="Guan Yu">Guan Yu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200951_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200951-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>These policies were the background for those implemented by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_China" title="Communist Party of China">Communist Party</a> after winning the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a> and taking power in 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200951_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200951-54">[54]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a>, between 1966 and 1976 of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China_(1949%E2%80%9376)" title="History of the People's Republic of China (1949–76)">Chairman Mao period</a> in the PRC, was the most serious and last systematic effort to destroy the ancient Chinese religion, while in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> the ancient Chinese religion was very well-preserved but controlled by <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Republic_of_China" title="President of the Republic of China">Republic of China (Taiwan) president</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chiang_Kai-Shek" title="Chiang Kai-Shek">Chiang Kai-Shek</a> during his <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Cultural_Renaissance" title="Chinese Cultural Renaissance">Chinese Cultural Renaissance</a> to counter the Cultural Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20139_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20139-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200951_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200951-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>After 1978 the ancient Chinese religion started to rapidly revive in China,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20131_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20131-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20138_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20138-56">[56]</a></sup> with millions of temples being rebuilt or built from scratch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20138_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20138-56">[56]</a></sup> Since the 1980s the central government moved to a policy of benign neglect or <i><a href="/wiki/Wu_wei" title="Wu wei">wu wei</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">無為</span>) in regard to rural community life, and the local government's new regulatory relationship with local society is characterised by practical mutual dependence; these factors have given much space for popular religion to develop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20138_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20138-56">[56]</a></sup> In recent years, in some cases, local governments have taken an even positive and supportive attitude towards indigenous religion in the name of promoting cultural heritage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20138_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20138-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200952_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvermyer200952-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Instead of signaling the demise of traditional ancient religion, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>'s economic and technological industrialization and development has brought a spiritual renewal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201328_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201328-58">[58]</a></sup> As its images and practices integrate the codes of the ancient Chinese culture, the ancient Chinese religion provides the <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> people in both <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> a means to face the challenges of modernisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201328_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201328-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Texts">Texts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Texts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_classics" title="Chinese classics">Chinese classics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E5%BF%A0%E7%B8%A3%E4%B8%81%E6%88%BF%E9%9B%99%E9%97%9502.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="541" data-file-width="758" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/%E5%BF%A0%E7%B8%A3%E4%B8%81%E6%88%BF%E9%9B%99%E9%97%9502.jpg/220px-%E5%BF%A0%E7%B8%A3%E4%B8%81%E6%88%BF%E9%9B%99%E9%97%9502.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/%E5%BF%A0%E7%B8%A3%E4%B8%81%E6%88%BF%E9%9B%99%E9%97%9502.jpg/330px-%E5%BF%A0%E7%B8%A3%E4%B8%81%E6%88%BF%E9%9B%99%E9%97%9502.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/%E5%BF%A0%E7%B8%A3%E4%B8%81%E6%88%BF%E9%9B%99%E9%97%9502.jpg/440px-%E5%BF%A0%E7%B8%A3%E4%B8%81%E6%88%BF%E9%9B%99%E9%97%9502.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E5%BF%A0%E7%B8%A3%E4%B8%81%E6%88%BF%E9%9B%99%E9%97%9502.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Han" title="Eastern Han">Eastern Han</a> (25–220 AD) Chinese stone-carved <a href="/wiki/Que_(tower)" title="Que (tower)">que pillar gates</a> of Dingfang, <a href="/wiki/Zhong_County" title="Zhong County">Zhong County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chongqing" title="Chongqing">Chongqing</a> that once belonged to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_temple" title="Chinese temple">temple</a> dedicated to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warring_States" title="Warring States">Warring States</a> era general <a href="/wiki/Ba_Manzi" title="Ba Manzi">Ba Manzi</a></div></div></div>
<p>Ancient Chinese religion draws from a vast heritage of sacred books, which according to the general worldview treat <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>, history and mythology, mysticism and philosophy, as aspects of the same thing. Historically, the revolutionary shift toward a preference for textual transmission and text-based knowledge over long-standing oral traditions first becomes detectable in the 1st century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJansen2012288_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJansen2012288-59">[59]</a></sup> The spoken word, however, never lost its power. Rather than writing replacing the power of the spoken word, both existed side by side. Scriptures had to be recited and heard in order to be efficacious, and the limitations of written texts were acknowledged particularly in <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a> and folk religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJansen2012289_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJansen2012289-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_classics" title="Chinese classics">classic books</a> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">經</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">jīng</span></i>; <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></small> '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warp_(weaving)" title="Warp (weaving)">warp</a>') such as the <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucian</a> canon including the "<a href="/wiki/Four_Books_and_Five_Classics" title="Four Books and Five Classics">Four Books and Five Classics</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">《四書五經》</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">sìshū wǔjīng</span></i>) and the "<a href="/wiki/Classic_of_Filial_Piety" title="Classic of Filial Piety">Classic of Filial Piety</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">《孝經》</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">xiàojīng</span></i>), then there are the <i><a href="/wiki/Mozi_(book)" title="Mozi (book)">Mozi</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Mohism" title="Mohism">Mohism</a>), the <i><a href="/wiki/Huainanzi" title="Huainanzi">Huainanzi</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Shizi_(book)" title="Shizi (book)">Shizi</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Xunzi_(book)" title="Xunzi (book)">Xunzi</a></i>. The "<a href="/wiki/Interactions_Between_Heaven_and_Mankind" title="Interactions Between Heaven and Mankind">Interactions Between Heaven and Mankind</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">《天人感應》</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">tiānrén gǎnyìng</span></i>) is a set of Confucianised doctrines compiled in the Han dynasty by <a href="/wiki/Dong_Zhongshu" title="Dong Zhongshu">Dong Zhongshu</a>, discussing politics in accordance with a personal <i><a href="/wiki/Tian" title="Tian">Tian</a></i> of whom mankind is viewed as the incarnation.
</p><p>Taoism has a separate body of philosophical, theological and ritual literature, including the fundamental <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daodejing" title="Daodejing">Daodejing</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">《道德經》</span>; <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></small> 'Book of the Way and its Virtue'), the <i><a href="/wiki/Daozang" title="Daozang">Daozang</a></i> (Taoist Canon), the <i><a href="/wiki/Liezi" title="Liezi">Liezi</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Zhuangzi_(book)" title="Zhuangzi (book)">Zhuangzi</a></i>, and a great number of other texts either included or not within the Taoist Canon. Vernacular literature and the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_salvationist_religions" title="Chinese salvationist religions">folk religious sects</a> have produced a great body of popular mythological and theological literature, the <i><a href="/wiki/Baojuan" title="Baojuan">baojuan</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">寶卷</span>; <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></small> 'precious scrolls').
</p><p>Recent discovery of ancient books, such as the "<a href="/wiki/Guodian_Chu_Slips" title="Guodian Chu Slips">Guodian texts</a>" in the 1990s and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huangdi_sijing" title="Huangdi sijing">Huangdi sijing</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">《黃帝四經》</span>; <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></small> 'Four Books of the Yellow Emperor') in the 1970s, has given rise to new interpretations of the ancient  Chinese religion and new directions in its post-Maoist renewal. Many of these books overcome the dichotomy between Confucian and Taoist traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> The Guodian texts include, among others, the <i><a href="/wiki/Taiyi_Shengshui" title="Taiyi Shengshui">Taiyi Shengshui</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">《太一生水》</span>; <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></small> 'The Great One Gives Birth to Water'). Another book attributed to the Yellow Emperor is the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huangdi_yinfujing" title="Huangdi yinfujing">Huangdi yinfujing</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">《黃帝陰符經》</span>; <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></small> '"Yellow Emperor's Book of the Hidden Symbol"').
</p><p>Classical books of mythology include the "<a href="/wiki/Classic_of_Mountains_and_Seas" title="Classic of Mountains and Seas">Classic of Mountains and Seas</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">《山海經》</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin"><i>shānhǎijīng</i></span></i>), the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Record_of_Heretofore_Lost_Works" title="Record of Heretofore Lost Works">Record of Heretofore Lost Works</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">《拾遺記</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">shíyíjì</span></i>), "<a href="/wiki/The_Peach_Blossom_Spring" title="The Peach Blossom Spring">The Peach Blossom Spring</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">《桃花源記》</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">táohuāyuánjì</span></i>), the "<a href="/wiki/Investiture_of_the_Gods" title="Investiture of the Gods">Investiture of the Gods</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">《封神演義》</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">fēngshén yǎnyì</span></i>), and the "<a href="/wiki/Journey_to_the_West" title="Journey to the West">Journey to the West</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">《西遊記》</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">xīyóujì</span></i>) among others.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Core_concepts_of_theology_and_cosmology">Core concepts of theology and cosmology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Core concepts of theology and cosmology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_theology" title="Chinese theology">Chinese theology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">Chinese philosophy</a></div>
<p>Fan and Chen summarise four spiritual, cosmological, and moral concepts:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20135–6_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20135–6-10">[10]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Tian" title="Tian">Tian</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">天</span>), Heaven, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_(philosophy)" title="Absolute (philosophy)">source</a> of moral meaning; <i><a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">qi</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">氣</span>), the breath or substance of which all things are made; the practice of <i><a href="/wiki/Ancestor_veneration_in_China" title="Ancestor veneration in China">jingzu</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">敬祖</span>), the veneration of ancestors; <i><a href="/wiki/Bao_ying" title="Bao ying">bao ying</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">報應</span>), moral reciprocity.
</p>
<h3><span id="Tian.2C_its_li_and_qi"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Tian,_its_li_and_qi"><i>Tian</i>, its <i>li</i> and <i>qi</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Tian, its li and qi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Tian" title="Tian">Tian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">Qi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tian,_god_of_the_square_of_the_north_astral_pole.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="486" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Tian%2C_god_of_the_square_of_the_north_astral_pole.svg/220px-Tian%2C_god_of_the_square_of_the_north_astral_pole.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Tian%2C_god_of_the_square_of_the_north_astral_pole.svg/330px-Tian%2C_god_of_the_square_of_the_north_astral_pole.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Tian%2C_god_of_the_square_of_the_north_astral_pole.svg/440px-Tian%2C_god_of_the_square_of_the_north_astral_pole.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tian,_god_of_the_square_of_the_north_astral_pole.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tian or Di as the square of the <a href="/wiki/Circumpolar_star" title="Circumpolar star">north astral pole</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><br/>"Tian is <i>dian</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">顛</span> ('top'), the highest and unexceeded. It derives from the characters <i>yi</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">一</span>, 'one', and <i>da</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">大</span>, 'big'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[note 1]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Confucians, Taoists, and other schools of thought share basic concepts of <i>Tian</i>. <i>Tian</i> is both the physical heavens, the home of the sun, moon, and stars, and also the home of the gods and ancestors. <i>Tian</i> by extension is source of moral meaning, as seen in the political principle, the <a href="/wiki/Mandate_of_Heaven" title="Mandate of Heaven">Mandate of Heaven</a>, which holds that <i>Tian</i>, responding to human virtue, grants the imperial family the right to rule and withdraws it when the dynasty declines in virtue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> This creativity or virtue (<i><a href="/wiki/De_(Chinese)" title="De (Chinese)">de</a></i>) in humans is the potentiality to transcend the given conditions and act wisely and morally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler,_2011._p._5_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adler,_2011._p._5-72">[71]</a></sup> <i>Tian</i> is therefore both <a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(religion)" title="Transcendence (religion)">transcendent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Immanence" title="Immanence">immanent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler,_2011._p._5_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Adler,_2011._p._5-72">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Tian</i> is defined in many ways, with many names, the most widely known being <i>Tàidì</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">太帝</span> (the "Great Deity") and <i><a href="/wiki/Shangdi" title="Shangdi">Shàngdì</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">上帝</span> (the "Primordial Deity").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-names_of_Heaven_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-names_of_Heaven-76">[note 2]</a></sup> The concept of <i>Shangdi</i> is especially rooted in the tradition of the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>, which gave prominence to the worship of <a href="/wiki/Ancestor" title="Ancestor">ancestral gods</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural_hero" title="Cultural hero">cultural heroes</a>. The "Primordial Deity" or "Primordial Emperor" was considered to be embodied in the human realm as the lineage of imperial power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Libbrecht_2007._p._43_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Libbrecht_2007._p._43-77">[75]</a></sup> <i>Di</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">帝</span>) is a term meaning "deity" or "emperor" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Imperium" title="Imperium">imperator</a></i>, verb <i>im-perare</i>; "making from within"), used either as a name of the primordial god or as a title of natural gods,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup> describing a principle that exerts a fatherly dominance over what it produces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._64_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._64-79">[77]</a></sup> With the <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a>, that preferred a religion focused on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nature_god" title="Nature god">gods of nature</a>, <i>Tian</i> became a more abstract and impersonal idea of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Libbrecht_2007._p._43_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Libbrecht_2007._p._43-77">[75]</a></sup> A popular representation is the <a href="/wiki/Jade_Emperor" title="Jade Emperor">Jade Deity</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">玉帝</span> <i>Yùdì</i>) or Jade Emperor (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">玉皇</span> <i>Yùhuáng</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-king-emperor-shaman-axis_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-king-emperor-shaman-axis-83">[note 3]</a></sup> originally formulated by Taoists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._71_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._71-84">[81]</a></sup> According to classical theology he manifests in <a href="/wiki/Wufang_Shangdi" title="Wufang Shangdi">five primary forms</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">五方上帝</span> <i>Wǔfāng Shàngdì</i>, "Five Forms of the Highest Deity").
</p><p>The <i>qi</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">气</span> is the breath or substance of which all things are made, including inanimate matter, the living beings, thought and gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeiser199629_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeiser199629-86">[83]</a></sup> It is the continuum energy—matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler,_2011._p._21_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adler,_2011._p._21-87">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stephen_F._Teiser" title="Stephen F. Teiser">Stephen F. Teiser</a> (1996) translates it as "stuff" of "psychophysical stuff".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeiser199629_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeiser199629-86">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">Neo-Confucian</a> thinkers such as <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Xi" title="Zhu Xi">Zhu Xi</a> developed the idea of <i><a href="/wiki/Li_(Confucianism)" title="Li (Confucianism)">li</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">理</span>, the "reason", "order" of Heaven, that is to say the pattern through which the <i>qi</i> develops, that is the polarity of <i>yin</i> and <i>yang</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeiser199630_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeiser199630-88">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler,_2011._p._13_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adler,_2011._p._13-89">[86]</a></sup>  In <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a> the <i><a href="/wiki/Tao" title="Tao">Tao</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">道</span> ("Way") denotes in one concept both the impersonal absolute <i>Tian</i> and its order of manifestation (<i>li</i>).
</p>
<h3><span id="Yin_and_yang.E2.80.94gui_and_shen"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Yin_and_yang—gui_and_shen"><i>Yin</i> and <i>yang</i>—<i>gui</i> and <i>shen</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Yin and yang—gui and shen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Yin_and_yang" title="Yin and yang">Yin and yang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shen_(Chinese_religion)" title="Shen (Chinese religion)">Shen (Chinese religion)</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox" style="width:16em"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2"><small><i>Yīnyáng</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">陰陽</span> motifs</small></th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-image" colspan="2"><table style="border-collapse:collapse;text-align:center;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;margin:auto;width:160px"><tbody><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="width:80px;text-align:center;:"><div style="display:table-cell;vertical-align:middle;width:80px;height:80px;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nat%C3%BCrlich_gewachsenes_yin-yang-.jpg"><img alt="Natürlich gewachsenes yin-yang-.jpg" data-file-height="1085" data-file-width="1446" decoding="async" height="60" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Nat%C3%BCrlich_gewachsenes_yin-yang-.jpg/80px-Nat%C3%BCrlich_gewachsenes_yin-yang-.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Nat%C3%BCrlich_gewachsenes_yin-yang-.jpg/120px-Nat%C3%BCrlich_gewachsenes_yin-yang-.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Nat%C3%BCrlich_gewachsenes_yin-yang-.jpg/160px-Nat%C3%BCrlich_gewachsenes_yin-yang-.jpg 2x" width="80"/></a></div><div style="padding:1px"></div></td><td style="width:80px;text-align:center;:"><div style="display:table-cell;vertical-align:middle;width:80px;height:80px;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yinyang,_heaven_%2B_squared_earth_(%2B_circumpolar_seven_stars_%2B_mountain)_---_colour.svg"><img alt="Yinyang, heaven + squared earth (+ circumpolar seven stars + mountain) --- colour.svg" data-file-height="642" data-file-width="647" decoding="async" height="79" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Yinyang%2C_heaven_%2B_squared_earth_%28%2B_circumpolar_seven_stars_%2B_mountain%29_---_colour.svg/80px-Yinyang%2C_heaven_%2B_squared_earth_%28%2B_circumpolar_seven_stars_%2B_mountain%29_---_colour.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Yinyang%2C_heaven_%2B_squared_earth_%28%2B_circumpolar_seven_stars_%2B_mountain%29_---_colour.svg/120px-Yinyang%2C_heaven_%2B_squared_earth_%28%2B_circumpolar_seven_stars_%2B_mountain%29_---_colour.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Yinyang%2C_heaven_%2B_squared_earth_%28%2B_circumpolar_seven_stars_%2B_mountain%29_---_colour.svg/160px-Yinyang%2C_heaven_%2B_squared_earth_%28%2B_circumpolar_seven_stars_%2B_mountain%29_---_colour.svg.png 2x" width="80"/></a></div><div style="padding:1px"></div></td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-below" colspan="2" style="text-align:left">Yin and yang naturally formed in a log in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, and in a cosmological diagram as <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">地</span> <i><a href="/wiki/Di_(Chinese_concept)" title="Di (Chinese concept)">Dì</a></i> (a mountain growing to Heaven and a square as its order) and <i><a href="/wiki/Tian" title="Tian">Tiān</a></i> as the <a
