href="/wiki/Big_Dipper" title="Big Dipper">Big Dipper</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[note 4]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quanjunxi_Linggong_-_DSCF8614.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Quanjunxi_Linggong_-_DSCF8614.JPG/220px-Quanjunxi_Linggong_-_DSCF8614.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Quanjunxi_Linggong_-_DSCF8614.JPG/330px-Quanjunxi_Linggong_-_DSCF8614.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Quanjunxi_Linggong_-_DSCF8614.JPG/440px-Quanjunxi_Linggong_-_DSCF8614.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Quanjunxi_Linggong_-_DSCF8614.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">泉郡溪靈宮</span> <i>Quánjùnxī línggōng</i>, the "Numinous Palace by the Brook in the Land of Springs", in <a href="/wiki/Quanzhou" title="Quanzhou">Quanzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[note 5]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><i>Yin</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">陰</span> and <i>yang</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">陽</span>, whose root meanings respectively are "shady" and "sunny", or "dark" and "light", are modes of manifestation of the <i>qi</i>, not material things in themselves. Yin is the <i>qi</i> in its dense, dark, sinking, wet, condensing mode; yang denotes the light, and the bright, rising, dry, expanding modality. Described as <i><a href="/wiki/Taiji_(philosophy)" title="Taiji (philosophy)">Taiji</a></i> (the "Great Pole"), they represent the polarity and complementarity that enlivens the <a href="/wiki/Cosmos" title="Cosmos">cosmos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler,_2011._p._13_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Adler,_2011._p._13-89">[86]</a></sup> They can also be conceived as "disorder" and "order", "activity" or "passivity", with act (<i>yang</i>) usually preferred over receptiveness (<i>yin</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thien_Do,_2003,_pp._10-11_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Thien_Do,_2003,_pp._10-11-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The concept <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">神</span> "<i>shén</i>" (cognate of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">申</span> <i>shēn</i>, "extending, expanding"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler,_2011._p._16_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adler,_2011._p._16-93">[88]</a></sup>) is translated as "gods" or "spirits". There are <i>shén</i> of nature; gods who were once people, such as the warrior <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guan_Gong" title="Guan Gong">Guan Gong</a>; household gods, such as the <a href="/wiki/Kitchen_God" title="Kitchen God">Stove God</a>; as well as ancestral gods (<i>zu</i> or <i>zuxian</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler,_2011._p._14_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adler,_2011._p._14-94">[89]</a></sup> In the domain of humanity the <i>shen</i> is the "psyche", or the power or agency within humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeiser199631_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeiser199631-95">[90]</a></sup> They are intimately involved in the life of this world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeiser199631_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeiser199631-95">[90]</a></sup> As spirits of stars, mountains and streams, <i>shen</i> exert a direct influence on things, making phenomena appear and things grow or extend themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeiser199631_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeiser199631-95">[90]</a></sup> An early Chinese dictionary, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shuowen_jiezi" title="Shuowen jiezi">Shuowen jiezi</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Xu_Shen" title="Xu Shen">Xu Shen</a>, explains that they "are the spirits of Heaven" and they "draw out the ten thousand things".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeiser199631_95-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeiser199631-95">[90]</a></sup> As forces of growth the gods are regarded as <i>yang</i>, opposed to a <i>yin</i> class of entities called <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">鬼</span> "<i><a href="/wiki/Ghosts_in_Chinese_culture" title="Ghosts in Chinese culture">guǐ</a></i>" (cognate of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">歸</span> <i>guī</i>, "return, contraction"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler,_2011._p._16_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Adler,_2011._p._16-93">[88]</a></sup> chaotic beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeiser199632_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeiser199632-96">[91]</a></sup> A disciple of <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Xi" title="Zhu Xi">Zhu Xi</a> noted that "between Heaven and Earth there is no thing that does not consist of yin and yang, and there is no place where yin and yang are not found. Therefore, there is no place where gods and spirits do not exist".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeiser199632_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeiser199632-96">[91]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Chinese_dragon" title="Chinese dragon">dragon</a> is a symbol of <i>yang</i>, the principle of generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Libbrecht_2007._p._43_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-Libbrecht_2007._p._43-77">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucian</a> thought, the supreme God and its order and the multiplicity of <i>shen</i> are identified as one and the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zongqi_Cai,_2004._p._314_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zongqi_Cai,_2004._p._314-97">[92]</a></sup> In the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yizhuan" title="Yizhuan">Yizhuan</a></i>, a commentary to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yijing" title="Yijing">Yijing</a></i>, it is written that "one <i>yin</i> and one <i>yang</i> are called the Tao ... the unfathomable change of <i>yin</i> and <i>yang</i> is called <i>shen</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zongqi_Cai,_2004._p._314_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zongqi_Cai,_2004._p._314-97">[92]</a></sup> In other texts, with a tradition going back to the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han period</a>, the gods and spirits are explained to be names of <i>yin</i> and <i>yang</i>, forces of contraction and forces of growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zongqi_Cai,_2004._p._314_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zongqi_Cai,_2004._p._314-97">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>While in popular thought they have conscience and personality,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[93]</a></sup> Neo-Confucian scholars tended to rationalise them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[94]</a></sup> Zhu Xi wrote that they act according to the <i>li</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler,_2011._p._16_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-Adler,_2011._p._16-93">[88]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Zai" title="Zhang Zai">Zhang Zai</a> wrote that they are "the inherent potential (<i>liang neng</i>) of the two ways of <i>qi</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cheng_Yi_(philosopher)" title="Cheng Yi (philosopher)">Cheng Yi</a> said that they are "traces of the creative process".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler,_2011._p._16_93-3"><a href="#cite_note-Adler,_2011._p._16-93">[88]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chen_Chun" title="Chen Chun">Chen Chun</a> wrote that <i>shen</i> and <i>gui</i> are expansions and contractions, going and coming, of <i>yin</i> and <i>yang</i>—<i>qi</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler,_2011._p._16_93-4"><a href="#cite_note-Adler,_2011._p._16-93">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Hun_and_po.2C_and_zu_and_xian"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hun_and_po,_and_zu_and_xian"><i>Hun</i> and <i>po</i>, and <i>zu</i> and <i>xian</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Hun and po, and zu and xian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Temple_of_the_Filial_Blessing_in_Ouhai,_Wenzhou,_Zhejiang,_China_(2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Temple_of_the_Filial_Blessing_in_Ouhai%2C_Wenzhou%2C_Zhejiang%2C_China_%282%29.jpg/220px-Temple_of_the_Filial_Blessing_in_Ouhai%2C_Wenzhou%2C_Zhejiang%2C_China_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Temple_of_the_Filial_Blessing_in_Ouhai%2C_Wenzhou%2C_Zhejiang%2C_China_%282%29.jpg/330px-Temple_of_the_Filial_Blessing_in_Ouhai%2C_Wenzhou%2C_Zhejiang%2C_China_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Temple_of_the_Filial_Blessing_in_Ouhai%2C_Wenzhou%2C_Zhejiang%2C_China_%282%29.jpg/440px-Temple_of_the_Filial_Blessing_in_Ouhai%2C_Wenzhou%2C_Zhejiang%2C_China_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Temple_of_the_Filial_Blessing_in_Ouhai,_Wenzhou,_Zhejiang,_China_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Temple of the Filial Blessing (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">孝佑宮</span> <i>Xiàoyòugōng</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Ouhai_District" title="Ouhai District">Ouhai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wenzhou" title="Wenzhou">Wenzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a>. It is a place for the worship of ancestors.</div></div></div>
<p>Like all things in matter, the human soul is characterised by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dialectics" title="Dialectics">dialectic</a> of <i>yang</i> and <i>yin</i>. These correspond to the <a href="/wiki/Hun_and_po" title="Hun and po"><i>hun</i> and <i>po</i></a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">魂魄</span>) respectively. The <i>hun</i> is the traditionally "masculine", <i>yang</i>, rational soul or mind, and the <i>po</i> is the traditionally "feminine", <i>yin</i>, animal soul that is associated with the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[96]</a></sup> <i>Hun</i> (mind) is the soul (<i>shen</i>) that gives a form to the vital breath (<i>qi</i>) of humans, and it develops through the <i>po</i>, stretching and moving intelligently in order to grasp things.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[97]</a></sup> The <i>po</i> is the soul (<i>shen</i>) which controls the physiological and psychological activities of humans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._69_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._69-103">[98]</a></sup> while the <i>hun</i>, the <i>shen</i> attached to the vital breath (<i>qi</i>), is the soul (<i>shen</i>) that is totally independent of corporeal substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._69_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._69-103">[98]</a></sup> The <i>hun</i> is independent and perpetual, and as such it never allows itself to be limited in matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._69_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._69-103">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[note 6]</a></sup> Otherwise said, the <i>po</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Di_(Chinese_concept)" title="Di (Chinese concept)">"earthly" (<i>di</i>)</a> soul that goes downward, while the <i>hun</i> is the "heavenly" (<i>tian</i>) soul that moves upward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeiser199631_95-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeiser199631-95">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>To extend life to its full potential the human <i>shen</i> must be cultivated, resulting in ever clearer, more luminous states of being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Teiser,_1996_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Teiser,_1996-14">[14]</a></sup> It can transform in the pure intelligent breath of deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._69_103-4"><a href="#cite_note-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._69-103">[98]</a></sup> In the human psyche there's no distinction between rationality and intuition, thinking and feeling: the human being is <i>xin</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">心</span>), mind-heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler,_2011._p._21_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Adler,_2011._p._21-87">[84]</a></sup> With death, while the <i>po</i> returns to the earth and disappears, the <i>hun</i> is thought to be pure awareness or <i>qi</i>, and is the <i>shen</i> to whom ancestral sacrifices are dedicated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>shen</i> of men who are properly cultivated and honoured after their death are upheld <a href="/wiki/Ancestor" title="Ancestor">ancestors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Progenitor" title="Progenitor">progenitors</a> (<i>zuxian</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">祖先</span> or simply <i>zu</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">祖</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler,_2011._p._14_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Adler,_2011._p._14-94">[89]</a></sup> When ancestries aren't properly cultivated the world falls into disruption, and they become <i>gui</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler,_2011._p._14_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-Adler,_2011._p._14-94">[89]</a></sup> Ancestral worship is intertwined with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Totemism" title="Totemism">totemism</a>, as the earliest ancestors of an ethnic lineage are often represented as animals or associated to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang,_2004._pp._60-61_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wang,_2004._pp._60-61-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ancestors are means of connection with the <i><a href="/wiki/Tian" title="Tian">Tian</a></i>, the primordial god which does not have form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang,_2004._pp._60-61_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wang,_2004._pp._60-61-40">[40]</a></sup> As ancestors have form, they shape the destiny of humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang,_2004._pp._60-61_40-3"><a href="#cite_note-Wang,_2004._pp._60-61-40">[40]</a></sup> Ancestors who have had a significant impact in shaping the destiny of large groups of people, creators of genetic lineages or spiritual traditions, and historical leaders who have invented crafts and institutions for the wealth of the Chinese nation (<a href="/wiki/Culture_hero" title="Culture hero">culture heroes</a>), are exalted among the highest divine manifestations or immortal beings (<i><a href="/wiki/Xian_(Taoism)" title="Xian (Taoism)">xian</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">仙</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010162,_165_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010162,_165-107">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>In fact, in the Chinese tradition there is no distinction between gods (<i>shen</i>) and immortal beings (<i>xian</i>), transcendental principles and their bodily manifestations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010158–161_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010158–161-108">[102]</a></sup> Gods can incarnate with a human form and human beings can reach higher spiritual states by the right way of action, that is to say by emulating the order of Heaven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010159_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010159-109">[103]</a></sup> Humans are considered one of the three aspects of a trinity (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">三才</span> <i>Sāncái</i>, "Three Powers"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010162–164_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010162–164-110">[104]</a></sup> the three foundations of all being; specifically, men are the medium between Heaven that engenders order and forms and Earth which receives and nourishes them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010162–164_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010162–164-110">[104]</a></sup> Men are endowed with the role of completing creation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010162–164_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010162–164-110">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sancai_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sancai-112">[note 7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bao_ying_and_ming_yun"><i>Bao ying</i> and <i>ming yun</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Bao ying and ming yun">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bao_ying" title="Bao ying">Bao ying</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ming_yun" title="Ming yun">Ming yun</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E5%AE%89%E5%B9%B3%E5%A4%A9%E5%90%8E%E5%AE%AE%E7%9F%B3%E5%B0%87%E8%BB%8D.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/%E5%AE%89%E5%B9%B3%E5%A4%A9%E5%90%8E%E5%AE%AE%E7%9F%B3%E5%B0%87%E8%BB%8D.JPG/220px-%E5%AE%89%E5%B9%B3%E5%A4%A9%E5%90%8E%E5%AE%AE%E7%9F%B3%E5%B0%87%E8%BB%8D.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/%E5%AE%89%E5%B9%B3%E5%A4%A9%E5%90%8E%E5%AE%AE%E7%9F%B3%E5%B0%87%E8%BB%8D.JPG/330px-%E5%AE%89%E5%B9%B3%E5%A4%A9%E5%90%8E%E5%AE%AE%E7%9F%B3%E5%B0%87%E8%BB%8D.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/%E5%AE%89%E5%B9%B3%E5%A4%A9%E5%90%8E%E5%AE%AE%E7%9F%B3%E5%B0%87%E8%BB%8D.JPG/440px-%E5%AE%89%E5%B9%B3%E5%A4%A9%E5%90%8E%E5%AE%AE%E7%9F%B3%E5%B0%87%E8%BB%8D.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E5%AE%89%E5%B9%B3%E5%A4%A9%E5%90%8E%E5%AE%AE%E7%9F%B3%E5%B0%87%E8%BB%8D.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Altar to the Stone Generals, protective deities, at the Kantai Tianhou Temple in <a href="/wiki/Anping_District" title="Anping District">Anping</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tainan" title="Tainan">Tainan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Chinese traditional concept of <i>bao ying</i> ("reciprocity", "retribution" or "judgement"), is inscribed in the cosmological view of an ordered world, in which all manifestations of being have an allotted span (<i>shu</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Destiny" title="Destiny">destiny</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010166_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010166-113">[106]</a></sup> and are rewarded according to the moral-cosmic quality of their actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201325_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201325-114">[107]</a></sup> It determines <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fate" title="Fate">fate</a>, as written in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhou_Dynasty" title="Zhou Dynasty">Zhou</a> texts: "on the doer of good, heaven sends down all blessings, and on the doer of evil, he sends down all calamities" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">書經•湯誥</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201326_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201326-115">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>The cosmic significance of <i>bao ying</i> is better understood by exploring other two traditional concepts of fate and meaning:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201321_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201321-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Ming_yun" title="Ming yun">Ming yun</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">命運</span>), the personal destiny or given condition of a being in his world, in which <i>ming</i> is "life" or "right", the given status of life, and <i>yun</i> defines both "circumstance" and "individual choice"; <i>ming</i> is given and influenced by the transcendent force <i>Tian</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">天</span>), that is the same as the "divine right" (<i>tianming</i>) of ancient rulers as identified by <a href="/wiki/Mencius" title="Mencius">Mencius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201321_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201321-11">[11]</a></sup> Personal destiny (<i>ming yun</i>) is thus perceived as both fixed (as life itself) and flexible, open-ended (since the individual can choose how to behave in <i>bao ying</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201321_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201321-11">[11]</a></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuan_fen" title="Yuan fen">Yuan fen</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">緣分</span>), "fateful <a href="/wiki/Coincidence" title="Coincidence">coincidence</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201323_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201323-12">[12]</a></sup> describing good and bad chances and potential relationships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201323_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201323-12">[12]</a></sup> Scholars K. S. Yang and D. Ho have analysed the psychological advantages of this belief: assigning causality of both negative and positive events to <i>yuan fen</i> reduces the conflictual potential of guilt and pride, and preserves social harmony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201324_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201324-116">[109]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p><i>Ming yun</i> and <i>yuan fen</i> are linked, because what appears on the surface to be chance (either positive or negative), is part of the deeper rhythm that shapes personal life based on how destiny is directed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201325_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201325-114">[107]</a></sup> Recognising this connection has the result of making a person responsible for his or her actions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201326_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201326-115">[108]</a></sup> doing good for others spiritually improves oneself and contributes to the harmony between men and environmental gods and thus to the wealth of a human community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201326–27_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201326–27-117">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>These three themes of the Chinese tradition—moral reciprocity, personal destiny, fateful coincidence—are completed by a fourth notion:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201327_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201327-118">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wu_(Chinese_religion)" title="Wu (Chinese religion)">Wu</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">悟</span>), "awareness" of <i>bao ying</i>. The awareness of one's own given condition inscribed in the ordered world produces responsibility towards oneself and others; awareness of <i>yuan fen</i> stirs to respond to events rather than resigning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201327_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201327-118">[111]</a></sup> Awareness may arrive as a gift, often unbidden, and then it evolves into a practice that the person intentionally follows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201327_118-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201327-118">[111]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>As part of the trinity of being (the Three Powers), humans are not totally submissive to spiritual force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010164_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010164-111">[105]</a></sup> While under the sway of spiritual forces, humans can actively engage with them, striving to change their own fate to prove the worth of their earthly life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010164_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010164-111">[105]</a></sup> In the Chinese traditional view of human destiny, the dichotomy between "fatalism" and "optimism" is overcome; human beings can shape their personal destiny to grasp their real worth in the transformation of the universe, seeing their place in the alliance with the gods and with Heaven to surpass the constraints of the physical body and mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010164_111-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010164-111">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Ling_and_xianling.E2.80.94holy_and_numen"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ling_and_xianling—holy_and_numen"><i>Ling</i> and <i>xianling</i>—holy and numen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Ling and xianling—holy and numen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ling_(Chinese_religion)" title="Ling (Chinese religion)">Ling (Chinese religion)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xian_ling" title="Xian ling">Xian ling</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ch-brahma-temple.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="637" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Ch-brahma-temple.jpg/220px-Ch-brahma-temple.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Ch-brahma-temple.jpg/330px-Ch-brahma-temple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Ch-brahma-temple.jpg/440px-Ch-brahma-temple.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ch-brahma-temple.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Temple of <a href="/wiki/Brahma" title="Brahma">Brahma</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simianshen" title="Simianshen">Simianshen</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">四面神</span> "Four-Faced God") in Chinese, in <a href="/wiki/Changhua" title="Changhua">Changhua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thai</a>-style worship of Simianshen has its origins among <a href="/wiki/Thai_Chinese" title="Thai Chinese">Thai Chinese</a>, and has spread over the last few decades among Mainland Chinese and <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Chinese" title="Overseas Chinese">Overseas Chinese</a> populations.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zhen-Wu_Temple_in_Wuci_Township.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1134" data-file-width="851" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Zhen-Wu_Temple_in_Wuci_Township.JPG/220px-Zhen-Wu_Temple_in_Wuci_Township.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Zhen-Wu_Temple_in_Wuci_Township.JPG/330px-Zhen-Wu_Temple_in_Wuci_Township.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Zhen-Wu_Temple_in_Wuci_Township.JPG/440px-Zhen-Wu_Temple_in_Wuci_Township.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zhen-Wu_Temple_in_Wuci_Township.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A shrine dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Xuanwu_(god)" title="Xuanwu (god)">Zhenwu</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wuqi_District" title="Wuqi District">Wuqi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taichung" title="Taichung">Taichung</a>, Taiwan.</div></div></div>
<p>In Chinese religion the concept of <i><a href="/wiki/Ling_(Chinese_religion)" title="Ling (Chinese religion)">ling</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">靈</span>) is the equivalent of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacred" title="Sacred">holy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Numen" title="Numen">numen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[112]</a></sup> <i>Shen</i> in the meaning of "spiritual" is a synonym.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeiser199632_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeiser199632-96">[91]</a></sup> The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yijing" title="Yijing">Yijing</a></i> states that "spiritual means not measured by yin and yang".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeiser199632_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeiser199632-96">[91]</a></sup> <i>Ling</i> is the state of the "medium" of the bivalency (<i>yin</i>-<i>yang</i>), and thus it is identical with the inchoate order of creation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thien_Do,_2003,_pp._10-11_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Thien_Do,_2003,_pp._10-11-15">[15]</a></sup> Things inspiring awe or wonder because they cannot be understood as either <i>yin</i> or <i>yang</i>, because they cross or disrupt the polarity and therefore cannot be conceptualised, are regarded as numinous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeiser199632_96-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeiser199632-96">[91]</a></sup> Entities possessing unusual spiritual characteristics, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albino" title="Albino">albino</a> members of a species, beings that are part animal part human, or people who die in unusual ways such as suicide or on battlefields, are considered numinous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeiser199632_96-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeiser199632-96">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>The notion of <i>xian ling</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">顯靈</span>), variously translated as "divine efficacy, virtue" or simply the "numen", is important for the relationship between people and gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[113]</a></sup> It describes the manifestation, activity, of the power of a god (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">靈氣</span> <i>ling qi</i>, "divine energy" or "effervescence"), the evidence of the holy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183-184_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183-184-121">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <i>xian ling</i> may be interpreted as the god revealing their <a href="/wiki/Divine_presence" title="Divine presence">presence</a> in a particular area and temple,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184-122">[115]</a></sup> through events that are perceived as extraordinary, <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">miraculous</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184-122">[115]</a></sup> Divine power usually manifests in the presence of a wide public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184_122-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184-122">[115]</a></sup> The "value" of human deities (<i>xian</i>) is judged according to their efficacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010168_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010168-123">[116]</a></sup> The perceived effectiveness of a deity to protect or bless also determines how much they should be worshipped, how big a temple should be built in their honour, and what position in the broader pantheon they would attain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010168_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010168-123">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zavidovskaya (2012) has studied how the incentive of temple restorations since the 1980s in <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_southern_China" title="Northern and southern China">northern China</a> was triggered by numerous alleged instances of gods becoming "active" and "returning", reclaiming their temples and place in society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184_122-3"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184-122">[115]</a></sup> She mentions the example of a Chenghuang Temple in <a href="/wiki/Yulin,_Shaanxi" title="Yulin, Shaanxi">Yulin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a>, that was turned into a <a href="/wiki/Granary" title="Granary">granary</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a>; it was restored to its original function in the 1980s after seeds stored within were always found to have rotted. This phenomenon, which locals attributed to the god Chenghuang, was taken a sign to empty his residence of grain and allow him back in.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184_122-4"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184-122">[115]</a></sup> The <i>ling qi</i>, divine energy, is believed to accumulate in certain places, temples, making them <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy" title="Holy">holy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184_122-5"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184-122">[115]</a></sup> Temples with a longer history are considered holier than newly built ones, which still need to be filled by divine energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184_122-6"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184-122">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another example Zavidovskaya cites is the cult of the god Zhenwu in Congluo Yu, <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._185_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._185-124">[117]</a></sup> the god's temples were in ruins and the cult inactive until the mid 1990s, when a man with terminal cancer, in his last hope prayed (<i>bai</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">拜</span>) to Zhenwu. The man began to miraculously recover each passing day, and after a year he was completely healed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._185_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._185-124">[117]</a></sup> As thanksgiving, he organised an opera performance in the god's honour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._185_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._185-124">[117]</a></sup> A temporary altar with a statue of Zhenwu and a stage for the performance were set up in an open space at the foot of a mountain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._185_124-3"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._185-124">[117]</a></sup> During the course of the opera, large white snakes appeared, passive and unafraid of the people, seemingly watching the opera; the snakes were considered by locals to be incarnations of Zhenwu, come to watch the opera held in his honour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._185_124-4"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._185-124">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Within temples, it is common to see banners bearing the phrase "if the heart is sincere, the god will reveal their power" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">心誠神靈</span> <i>xin cheng shen ling</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183-125">[118]</a></sup> The relationship between people and gods is an exchange of favour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183-125">[118]</a></sup> This implies the belief that gods respond to the entreaties of the believer if their religious fervour is sincere (<i>cheng xin</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">誠心</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183-125">[118]</a></sup> If a person believes in the god's power with all their heart and expresses piety, the gods are confident in their faith and reveal their efficacious power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183_125-3"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183-125">[118]</a></sup> At the same time, for faith to strengthen in the devotee's heart, the deity has to prove their efficacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183_125-4"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183-125">[118]</a></sup> In exchange for divine favours, a faithful honours the deity with vows (<i>huan yuan</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">還願</span> or <i>xu yuan</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">許願</span>), through individual worship, reverence and respect (<i>jing shen</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">敬神</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183_125-5"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183-125">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most common display of divine power is the cure of diseases after a believer piously requests aid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184_122-7"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184-122">[115]</a></sup> Another manifestation is granting a request of children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184_122-8"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184-122">[115]</a></sup> The deity may also manifest through mediumship, entering the body of a shaman-medium and speaking through them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184_122-9"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184-122">[115]</a></sup> There have been cases of people curing illnesses "on behalf of a god" (<i>ti shen zhi bing</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">替神治病</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._185_124-5"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._185-124">[117]</a></sup> Gods may also speak to people when they are asleep (<i>tuomeng</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">託夢</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184_122-10"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._184-122">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sociological_typology">Sociological typology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Sociological typology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Wu Hsin-Chao (2014) distinguishes four kinds of Chinese traditional religious organisation:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWu201411,_and_note_1_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWu201411,_and_note_1-126">[119]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_ancestral_religion" title="Chinese ancestral religion">ancestry worship</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_communal_deity_religion" title="Chinese communal deity religion">deity worship</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_secret_societies" title="Chinese secret societies">secret societies</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_salvationist_religions" title="Chinese salvationist religions">folk religious sects</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Types_of_indigenous.E2.80.94ethnic_religion"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_indigenous—ethnic_religion">Types of indigenous—ethnic religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Types of indigenous—ethnic religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Worship_of_local_and_national_deities">Worship of local and national deities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Worship of local and national deities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zhangzhou_Putou_Damiao_20120225-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3168" data-file-width="4752" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Zhangzhou_Putou_Damiao_20120225-2.jpg/220px-Zhangzhou_Putou_Damiao_20120225-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Zhangzhou_Putou_Damiao_20120225-2.jpg/330px-Zhangzhou_Putou_Damiao_20120225-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Zhangzhou_Putou_Damiao_20120225-2.jpg/440px-Zhangzhou_Putou_Damiao_20120225-2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zhangzhou_Putou_Damiao_20120225-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">浦頭大廟</span> <i>Pǔtóu dàmiào</i>, the "First Great Temple by the Riverside", in <a href="/wiki/Zhangzhou" title="Zhangzhou">Zhangzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Chinese religion in its communal expression involves the worship of gods that are the generative power and tutelary spirit (<i><a href="/wiki/Genius_loci" title="Genius loci">genius loci</a></i>) of a locality or a certain aspect of nature (for example <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_gods" title="Water gods">water gods</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_gods" title="River gods">river gods</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fire_gods" title="Fire gods">fire gods</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mountain_gods" title="Mountain gods">mountain gods</a>), or of gods that are common ancestors of a village, a larger identity, or the Chinese nation (<a href="/wiki/Shennong" title="Shennong">Shennong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Emperor" title="Yellow Emperor">Huangdi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pangu" title="Pangu">Pangu</a>).
</p><p>The social structure of this religion is the <i>shénshè</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">神社</span> (literally "society of a god"), synonymous with <i>shehui</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">社會</span>, in which <i>shè</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">社</span> originally meant the altar of a community's earth god,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvermyer2009xii_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvermyer2009xii-127">[120]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">會</span> <i>huì</i> means "association", "assembly", "church" or "gathering". This type of religious trusts can be dedicated to a god which is bound to a single village or temple or to a god which has a wider following, in multiple villages, provinces or even a national importance. <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a> distinguished "god associations", "village communities" and "temple associations" in his analysis of religious trusts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mao-hui_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mao-hui-128">[121]</a></sup> In his words: "every kind and type of god [<i>shen</i>] can have an association [<i>hui</i>]", for example the Zhaogong Association, the Guanyin Association, the Guangong Association, the Dashen Association, the Bogong Association, the Wenchang Association, and the like.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mao-hui_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mao-hui-128">[121]</a></sup> Within the category of <i>hui</i> Mao also distinguished the sacrifice associations (<i>jiàohuì</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">醮會</span>) which make sacrifices in honour of gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mao-hui_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mao-hui-128">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>These societies organise gatherings and festivals (<i><a href="/wiki/Miaohui" title="Miaohui">miaohui</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">廟會</span>) participated by members of the whole village or larger community on the occasions of what are believed to be the birthdays of the gods or other events,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20138_56-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20138-56">[56]</a></sup> or to seek protection from droughts, epidemics, and other disasters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20138_56-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20138-56">[56]</a></sup> Such festivals invoke the power of the gods for practical goals to "summon blessings and drive away harm".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20138_56-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20138-56">[56]</a></sup> Special devotional currents within this framework can be identified by specific names such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazuism" title="Mazuism">Mazuism</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">媽祖教</span> <i>Māzǔjiào</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wang_Ye_worship" title="Wang Ye worship">Wang Ye worship</a>, or the cult of the Silkworm Mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>This type of religion is prevalent in north China, where lineage religion is absent, private, or historically present only within families of southern origin, and patrilineal ties are based on <a href="/wiki/Agnatic_seniority" title="Agnatic seniority">seniority</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChau2005a50_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChau2005a50-131">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu20_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wu20-132">[125]</a></sup> and villages are composed of people with different surnames. In this context, the deity societies or temple societies function as poles of the civil organism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overmyer,_2009._pp._12-13_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Overmyer,_2009._pp._12-13-133">[126]</a></sup> Often deity societies incorporate entire villages; this is the reason why in north China can be found many villages which are named after deities and their temples, for example <i>Léishénmiào</i> village (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">雷神廟</span> "[Village of the] Temple of the Thunder God")  or <i>Mǎshénmiàocūn</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">馬神廟村</span> "Village of the Temple of the Horse God").
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lineage_religion">Lineage religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Lineage religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_ancestral_religion" title="Chinese ancestral religion">Chinese ancestral religion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guanji_temple_and_Huang_shrine_in_Lucheng,_Wenzhou,_Zhejiang,_China_(1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="666" data-file-width="824" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Guanji_temple_and_Huang_shrine_in_Lucheng%2C_Wenzhou%2C_Zhejiang%2C_China_%281%29.jpg/220px-Guanji_temple_and_Huang_shrine_in_Lucheng%2C_Wenzhou%2C_Zhejiang%2C_China_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Guanji_temple_and_Huang_shrine_in_Lucheng%2C_Wenzhou%2C_Zhejiang%2C_China_%281%29.jpg/330px-Guanji_temple_and_Huang_shrine_in_Lucheng%2C_Wenzhou%2C_Zhejiang%2C_China_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Guanji_temple_and_Huang_shrine_in_Lucheng%2C_Wenzhou%2C_Zhejiang%2C_China_%281%29.jpg/440px-Guanji_temple_and_Huang_shrine_in_Lucheng%2C_Wenzhou%2C_Zhejiang%2C_China_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guanji_temple_and_Huang_shrine_in_Lucheng,_Wenzhou,_Zhejiang,_China_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Guanji <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_temple" title="Chinese temple">temple</a> (left) and Huang <a href="/wiki/Ancestral_shrine" title="Ancestral shrine">ancestral shrine</a> (right) in <a href="/wiki/Wenzhou" title="Wenzhou">Wenzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Worship_at_an_ancestral_temple_in_Hong%27an,_Hubei,_China.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="389" data-file-width="588" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Worship_at_an_ancestral_temple_in_Hong%27an%2C_Hubei%2C_China.jpg/220px-Worship_at_an_ancestral_temple_in_Hong%27an%2C_Hubei%2C_China.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Worship_at_an_ancestral_temple_in_Hong%27an%2C_Hubei%2C_China.jpg/330px-Worship_at_an_ancestral_temple_in_Hong%27an%2C_Hubei%2C_China.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Worship_at_an_ancestral_temple_in_Hong%27an%2C_Hubei%2C_China.jpg/440px-Worship_at_an_ancestral_temple_in_Hong%27an%2C_Hubei%2C_China.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Worship_at_an_ancestral_temple_in_Hong%27an,_Hubei,_China.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>People gather for a worship ceremony at an ancestral shrine in <a href="/wiki/Hong%27an_County" title="Hong'an County">Hong'an</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hubei" title="Hubei">Hubei</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Another dimension of the Chinese folk religion is based on family or genealogical worship of deities and ancestors in family altars or private temples (<i>simiao</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">私廟</span> or <i>jiamiao</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">家廟</span>), or <a href="/wiki/Ancestral_shrine" title="Ancestral shrine">ancestral shrines</a> (<i>citang</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">祠堂</span> or <i>zongci</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">宗祠</span>, or also <i>zumiao</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">祖廟</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201313_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201313-134">[127]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_lineage_associations" title="Chinese lineage associations">Kinship associations or churches</a> (<i>zōngzú xiéhuì</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">宗族協會</span>), congregating people with the same <a href="/wiki/Chinese_surname" title="Chinese surname">surname</a> and belonging to the same <a href="/wiki/Chinese_kin" title="Chinese kin">kin</a>, are the social expression of this religion: these lineage societies build temples where the deified ancestors of a certain group (for example the <i><a href="/wiki/Chen_(surname)" title="Chen (surname)">Chens</a></i> or the <i><a href="/wiki/Lin_(surname)" title="Lin (surname)">Lins</a></i>) are enshrined and worshiped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201314–15_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201314–15-135">[128]</a></sup> These temples serve as centres of aggregation for people belonging to the same lineage, and the lineage body may provide a context of identification and mutual assistance for individual persons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201314–15_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201314–15-135">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>The construction of large and elaborate ancestral temples traditionally represents a kin's wealth, influence and achievement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201315_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201315-136">[129]</a></sup> Scholar K. S. Yang has explored the ethno-political dynamism of this form of religion, through which people who become distinguished for their value and virtue are considered immortal and receive posthumous divine titles, and are believed to protect their descendants, inspiring a mythological lore for the collective memory of a family or kin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201316_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201316-137">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>If their temples and their deities enshrined acquire popularity they are considered worthy of the virtue of <i>ling</i>, "efficacy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201316_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201316-137">[130]</a></sup> Worship of ancestors (<i><a href="/wiki/Ancestor_veneration_in_China" title="Ancestor veneration in China">jingzu</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">敬祖</span>) is observed nationally with large-scale rituals on <a href="/wiki/Qingming_Festival" title="Qingming Festival">Qingming Festival</a> and other holidays.
</p><p>This type of religion prevails in south China, where lineage bonds are stronger and the patrilineal hierarchy is not based upon seniority, and access to corporate resources held by a lineage is based upon the equality of all the lines of descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu20_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wu20-132">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophical_and_ritual_modalities">Philosophical and ritual modalities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Philosophical and ritual modalities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wuism_and_shamanic_traditions">Wuism and shamanic traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Wuism and shamanic traditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_shamanism" title="Chinese shamanism">Chinese shamanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuo_folk_religion" title="Nuo folk religion">Nuo folk religion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yard_leading_to_the_Temple_of_the_White_Sulde_of_Genghis_Khan,_in_Uxin,_Inner_Mongolia,_China.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Yard_leading_to_the_Temple_of_the_White_Sulde_of_Genghis_Khan%2C_in_Uxin%2C_Inner_Mongolia%2C_China.jpg/220px-Yard_leading_to_the_Temple_of_the_White_Sulde_of_Genghis_Khan%2C_in_Uxin%2C_Inner_Mongolia%2C_China.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Yard_leading_to_the_Temple_of_the_White_Sulde_of_Genghis_Khan%2C_in_Uxin%2C_Inner_Mongolia%2C_China.jpg/330px-Yard_leading_to_the_Temple_of_the_White_Sulde_of_Genghis_Khan%2C_in_Uxin%2C_Inner_Mongolia%2C_China.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Yard_leading_to_the_Temple_of_the_White_Sulde_of_Genghis_Khan%2C_in_Uxin%2C_Inner_Mongolia%2C_China.jpg/440px-Yard_leading_to_the_Temple_of_the_White_Sulde_of_Genghis_Khan%2C_in_Uxin%2C_Inner_Mongolia%2C_China.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yard_leading_to_the_Temple_of_the_White_Sulde_of_Genghis_Khan,_in_Uxin,_Inner_Mongolia,_China.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Temple of the <a href="/wiki/S%C3%BClde_Tngri" title="Sülde Tngri">White Sulde</a> of <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a> in the town of <a href="/wiki/Uxin_Banner" title="Uxin Banner">Uxin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Ordos_Desert" title="Ordos Desert">Mu Us Desert</a>. The worship of Genghis is shared by Chinese and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongolian_folk_religion" title="Mongolian folk religion">Mongolian folk religion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[note 8]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>"The extent to which shamanism pervaded ancient Chinese society", says scholar Paul R. Goldin (2005), "is a matter of scholarly dispute, but there can be no doubt that many communities relied upon the unique talents of shamans for their quotidian spiritual needs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[131]</a></sup> The Chinese usage distinguishes the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_shamanism" title="Chinese shamanism">Chinese <i>wu</i> tradition</a> or "Wuism" as it was called by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jan_Jakob_Maria_de_Groot" title="Jan Jakob Maria de Groot">Jan Jakob Maria de Groot</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Groot1892''passim''_vol._6_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Groot1892''passim''_vol._6-140">[132]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">巫教</span> <i>wūjiào</i>; properly shamanic, with control over the gods) from the <a href="/wiki/Tongji_(spirit_medium)" title="Tongji (spirit medium)"><i>tongji</i> tradition</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">童乩</span>; mediumship, without control of the godly movement), and from non-Han Chinese Altaic shamanisms (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">薩滿教</span> <i>sàmǎnjiào</i>) that are practised in northern provinces.
</p><p>According to Andreea Chirita (2014), Confucianism itself, with its emphasis on hierarchy and ancestral rituals, derived from the shamanic discourse of the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>. What Confucianism did was to marginalise the "dysfunctional" features of old shamanism. However, shamanic traditions continued uninterrupted within the folk religion and found precise and functional forms within Taoism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chirita2014_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chirita2014-141">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Shang and <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a>, shamans had a role in the political hierarchy, and were represented institutionally by the Ministry of Rites (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">大宗伯</span>). The emperor was considered the supreme shaman, intermediating between the three realms of heaven, earth and man. The mission of a shaman (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">巫</span> <i><a href="/wiki/Wu_(shaman)" title="Wu (shaman)">wu</a></i>) is "to repair the dis-functionalities occurred in nature and generated after the sky had been separated from earth":<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chirita2014_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chirita2014-141">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The female shamans called <i>wu</i> as well as the male shamans called <i>xi</i> represent the voice of spirits, repair the natural dis-functions, foretell the future based on dreams and the art of divination ... "a historical science of the future", whereas shamans are able to observe the yin and the yang ...</p></blockquote>
<p>Since the 1980s the practice and study of shamanism has undergone a massive revival in Chinese religion as a means to repair the world to a harmonious whole after industrialisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chirita2014_141-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chirita2014-141">[133]</a></sup> Shamanism is viewed by many scholars as the foundation for the emergence of civilisation, and the shaman as "teacher and spirit" of peoples. The Chinese Society for Shamanic Studies was founded in <a href="/wiki/Jilin_City" title="Jilin City">Jilin City</a> in 1988.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KunShi2006_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-KunShi2006-142">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nuo folk religion is a system of the Chinese folk religion with distinct institutions and cosmology present especially in central-southern China. It arose as an <a href="/wiki/Exorcism" title="Exorcism">exorcistic</a> religious movement, and it is interethnic but also intimately connected to the <a href="/wiki/Tujia_people" title="Tujia people">Tujia people</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELi2016_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELi2016-143">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Confucianism.2C_Taoism_and_orders_of_ritual_masters"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Confucianism,_Taoism_and_orders_of_ritual_masters">Confucianism, Taoism and orders of ritual masters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Confucianism, Taoism and orders of ritual masters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_ritual_mastery_traditions" title="Chinese ritual mastery traditions">Chinese ritual mastery traditions</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fushou_(Fortune_and_Longevity)_Taoist_Temple_at_Tianchi_(Heavenly_Lake)_in_Fukang,_Changji,_Xinjiang.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Fushou_%28Fortune_and_Longevity%29_Taoist_Temple_at_Tianchi_%28Heavenly_Lake%29_in_Fukang%2C_Changji%2C_Xinjiang.jpg/220px-Fushou_%28Fortune_and_Longevity%29_Taoist_Temple_at_Tianchi_%28Heavenly_Lake%29_in_Fukang%2C_Changji%2C_Xinjiang.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Fushou_%28Fortune_and_Longevity%29_Taoist_Temple_at_Tianchi_%28Heavenly_Lake%29_in_Fukang%2C_Changji%2C_Xinjiang.jpg/330px-Fushou_%28Fortune_and_Longevity%29_Taoist_Temple_at_Tianchi_%28Heavenly_Lake%29_in_Fukang%2C_Changji%2C_Xinjiang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Fushou_%28Fortune_and_Longevity%29_Taoist_Temple_at_Tianchi_%28Heavenly_Lake%29_in_Fukang%2C_Changji%2C_Xinjiang.jpg/440px-Fushou_%28Fortune_and_Longevity%29_Taoist_Temple_at_Tianchi_%28Heavenly_Lake%29_in_Fukang%2C_Changji%2C_Xinjiang.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fushou_(Fortune_and_Longevity)_Taoist_Temple_at_Tianchi_(Heavenly_Lake)_in_Fukang,_Changji,_Xinjiang.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Temple of Fortune and Longevity, at the <a href="/wiki/Heavenly_Lake_of_Tianshan" title="Heavenly Lake of Tianshan">Heavenly Lake of Tianshan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fukang" title="Fukang">Fukang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Changji_Hui_Autonomous_Prefecture" title="Changji Hui Autonomous Prefecture">Changji</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>. It is an example of Taoist temple which hosts various chapels dedicated to popular gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[note 9]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E9%9D%88%E5%AE%89%E5%A3%87%E5%A4%A7%E6%B3%95%E5%B8%AB.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/%E9%9D%88%E5%AE%89%E5%A3%87%E5%A4%A7%E6%B3%95%E5%B8%AB.JPG/220px-%E9%9D%88%E5%AE%89%E5%A3%87%E5%A4%A7%E6%B3%95%E5%B8%AB.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/%E9%9D%88%E5%AE%89%E5%A3%87%E5%A4%A7%E6%B3%95%E5%B8%AB.JPG/330px-%E9%9D%88%E5%AE%89%E5%A3%87%E5%A4%A7%E6%B3%95%E5%B8%AB.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/%E9%9D%88%E5%AE%89%E5%A3%87%E5%A4%A7%E6%B3%95%E5%B8%AB.JPG/440px-%E9%9D%88%E5%AE%89%E5%A3%87%E5%A4%A7%E6%B3%95%E5%B8%AB.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E9%9D%88%E5%AE%89%E5%A3%87%E5%A4%A7%E6%B3%95%E5%B8%AB.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Folk ritual masters conducting a ceremony.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jiangyin_wenmiao_dachengdian.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4500" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Jiangyin_wenmiao_dachengdian.jpg/220px-Jiangyin_wenmiao_dachengdian.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Jiangyin_wenmiao_dachengdian.jpg/330px-Jiangyin_wenmiao_dachengdian.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Jiangyin_wenmiao_dachengdian.jpg/440px-Jiangyin_wenmiao_dachengdian.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jiangyin_wenmiao_dachengdian.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Temple of the God of Culture (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">文廟</span> <i>wénmiào</i>) of <a href="/wiki/Jiangyin" title="Jiangyin">Jiangyin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wuxi" title="Wuxi">Wuxi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a>. In this temple the <i>Wéndì</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">文帝</span>, "God of Culture") enshrined is <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Confucianism and Taoism—which are formalised, ritual, doctrinal or philosophical traditions—can be considered both as embedded within the larger category of Chinese religion, or as separate religions. In fact, one can practise certain folk cults and espouse the tenets of Confucianism as a philosophical framework, Confucian theology instructing to uphold the moral order through the worship of gods and ancestors<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[136]</a></sup> that is the way of connecting to the <a href="/wiki/Tian" title="Tian">Tian</a> and awakening to its harmony (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Li_(Confucian)" title="Li (Confucian)">li</a></i>, "<a href="/wiki/%E1%B9%9Ata" title="Ṛta">rite</a>").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Folk temples and ancestral shrines on special occasions may choose Confucian liturgy (that is called <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">儒</span> <i>rú</i>, or sometimes <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">正統</span> <i>zhèngtǒng</i>, meaning "<a href="/wiki/Orthopraxy" title="Orthopraxy">orthoprax</a>" ritual style) led by Confucian "sages of rites" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">禮生</span> <i>lǐshēng</i>) who in many cases are the elders of a local community. Confucian liturgies are alternated with Taoist liturgies and popular ritual styles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClart20033–5_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClart20033–5-147">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are many organised groups of the folk religion that adopt Confucian liturgy and identity, for example the <a href="/wiki/Way_of_the_Gods_according_to_the_Confucian_Tradition" title="Way of the Gods according to the Confucian Tradition">Way of the Gods according to the Confucian Tradition</a> or phoenix churches (Luanism), or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confucian_churches" title="Confucian churches">Confucian churches</a>, schools and fellowships such as the <i>Yīdān xuétáng</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">一耽學堂</span>) of <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[139]</a></sup> the <i>Mèngmǔtáng</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">孟母堂</span>) of <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fan,_Chen._2015._p._29_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fan,_Chen._2015._p._29-149">[140]</a></sup> the Confucian Fellowship (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">儒教道壇</span> <i>Rújiào Dàotán</i>) in northern Fujian, and ancestral temples of the Kong (Confucius) lineage operating as well as Confucian-teaching churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fan,_Chen._2015._p._29_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fan,_Chen._2015._p._29-149">[140]</a></sup> In November 2015 a national <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kongshenghui" title="Kongshenghui">Church of Confucius</a> was established with the contribution of many Confucian leaders.
</p><p>Scholar and Taoist priest <a href="/wiki/Kristofer_Schipper" title="Kristofer Schipper">Kristofer Schipper</a> defines Taoism as a "liturgical framework" for the development of local religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu2014_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wu2014-150">[141]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Taoist_schools" title="Taoist schools">currents</a> of Taoism are deeply interwoven with the Chinese folk religion, especially the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhengyi_Taoism" title="Zhengyi Taoism">Zhengyi</a> school, developing aspects of local cults within their doctrines;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu2014_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wu2014-150">[141]</a></sup> however Taoists always highlight the distinction between their traditions and those which are not Taoist. Priests of Taoism are called <i>daoshi</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">道士</span>), literally meaning "masters of the <a href="/wiki/Tao" title="Tao">Tao</a>", otherwise commonly translated simply the "Taoists", as common followers and folk believers who are not part of Taoist orders are not identified as such.
</p><p>Taoists of the Zhengyi school, who are called <i>sǎnjū dàoshi</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">散居道士</span>) or <i>huǒjū dàoshi</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">火居道士</span>), respectively meaning "scattered daoshi" and "daoshi living at home (hearth)", because they can get married and perform the profession of priests as a part-time occupation, may perform rituals of offering (<i>jiao</i>), thanks-giving, propitiation, exorcism and rites of passage for local communities' temples and private homes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis-Daoists_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davis-Daoists-151">[142]</a></sup> Local gods of local cultures are often incorporated into their altars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis-Daoists_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davis-Daoists-151">[142]</a></sup> The Zhengyi Taoists are trained by other priests of the same sect, and historically received formal ordination by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_Master" title="Celestial Master">Celestial Master</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pas,_2014._p._259_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pas,_2014._p._259-152">[143]</a></sup> although the 63rd Celestial Master Zhang Enpu fled to Taiwan in the 1940s during the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chinese_ritual_mastery_traditions" title="Chinese ritual mastery traditions">Lineages of ritual masters</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">法師</span> <i>fashi</i>), also referred to as practitioners of "Faism", also called "Folk Taoism" or (in southeast China) "Red Taoism", operate within the Chinese folk religion but outside any institution of official Taoism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pas,_2014._p._259_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pas,_2014._p._259-152">[143]</a></sup> The ritual masters, who have the same role of the <i>sanju daoshi</i> within the fabric of society, are not considered Taoist priests by the <i>daoshi</i> of Taoism who trace their lineage to the Celestial Masters and by Taoists officially registered with the state Taoist Church. <i>Fashi</i> are defined as of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kataphatic" title="Kataphatic">kataphatic</a>" (filling) character in opposition to professional Taoists who are "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kenotic" title="Kenotic">kenotic</a>" (of emptying, or <a href="/wiki/Apophatic_theology" title="Apophatic theology">apophatic</a>, character).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organised_folk_religious_sects">Organised folk religious sects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Organised folk religious sects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_salvationist_religions" title="Chinese salvationist religions">Chinese salvationist religions</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Main_temple_of_the_City_of_the_Eight_Symbols_(%E5%85%AB%E5%8D%A6%E5%9F%8E),_the_holy_see_of_Weixinism_(%E5%94%AF%E5%BF%83%E6%95%99)_in_Hebi_(%E9%B9%A4%E5%A3%81%E5%B8%82),_Henan,_China.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The City of the Eight Symbols in <a href="/wiki/Qi_County,_Hebi" title="Qi County, Hebi">Qi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hebi" title="Hebi">Hebi</a>, is the headquarters of the Weixinist Church in <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>China has a long history of sect traditions characterised by a <a href="/wiki/Soteriology" title="Soteriology">soteriological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eschatology" title="Eschatology">eschatological</a> character, often called "salvationist religions" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">救度宗教</span> <i>jiùdù zōngjiào</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer201119_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer201119-154">[145]</a></sup>  They emerged from the common religion but are not part of the lineage cult of <a href="/wiki/Ancestor" title="Ancestor">ancestors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Progenitor" title="Progenitor">progenitors</a>, nor the communal deity religion of village temples, neighbourhood, corporations , or national temples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer201119–20_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer201119–20-155">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Prasenjit_Duara" title="Prasenjit Duara">Prasenjit Duara</a> has termed them "redemptive societies" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">救世團體</span> <i>jiùshì tuántǐ</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer201117_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer201117-156">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClart2014395_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClart2014395-157">[148]</a></sup> while modern Chinese scholarship describes them as "folk religious sects" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">民間宗教</span> <i>mínjiān zōngjiào</i>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">民間教門</span> <i>mínjiān jiàomén</i> or <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">民间教派</span> <i>mínjiān jiàopài</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer201112_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer201112-158">[149]</a></sup> abandoning the derogatory term used by imperial officials, <i>xiéjiào</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">邪教</span>), "evil religion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer201123_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer201123-159">[150]</a></sup>
