</p><p>They are characterised by several elements, including <a href="/wiki/Egalitarianism" title="Egalitarianism">egalitarianism</a>; foundation by a charismatic figure; direct divine revelation; a <a href="/wiki/Millenarianism" title="Millenarianism">millenarian</a> eschatology and voluntary path of <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a>; an embodied experience of the numinous through healing and cultivation; and an expansive orientation through good deeds, <a href="/wiki/Evangelism" title="Evangelism">evangelism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philanthropy" title="Philanthropy">philanthropy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer201119_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer201119-154">[145]</a></sup> Their practices are focused on improving morality, body cultivation, and recitation of scriptures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer201119_154-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer201119-154">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many of the redemptive religions of the 20th and 21st century aspire to become the repository of the entirety of the Chinese tradition in the face of Western modernism and materialism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer201129_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer201129-160">[151]</a></sup> This group of religions includes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer20114–6_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer20114–6-161">[152]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yiguandao" title="Yiguandao">Yiguandao</a> and other sects belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Xiantiandao" title="Xiantiandao">Xiantiandao</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">先天道</span> "Way of Former Heaven"), Jiugongdao (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">九宮道</span> "Way of the Nine Palaces"), various proliferations of the <a href="/wiki/Luo_teaching" title="Luo teaching">Luo teaching</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Zaili_teaching" title="Zaili teaching">Zaili teaching</a>, and the more recent <a href="/wiki/De_teaching" title="De teaching">De teaching</a>, Weixinist, <a href="/wiki/Xuanyuan_teaching" title="Xuanyuan teaching">Xuanyuan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tiandi_teachings" title="Tiandi teachings">Tiandi teachings</a>, the latter two focused respectively on the worship of <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Emperor" title="Yellow Emperor">Huangdi</a> and the universal God. Also, the <a href="/wiki/Qigong" title="Qigong">qigong</a> schools are developments of the same religious context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer201111_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer201111-162">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>These folk sectarian offer different world views and compete for influence. To take one example, <a href="/wiki/Yiguandao" title="Yiguandao">Yiguandao</a> focuses on personal salvation through inner work and considers itself the most valid "Way of Heaven" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">天道</span> <i>Tiāndào</i>). Yiguandao offers its own "<a href="/wiki/Xiantiandao" title="Xiantiandao">Way of Former Heaven</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">先天道</span> <i>Xiāntiāndào</i>), that is, a cosmological definition of the state of things prior to creation, in unity with God. It regards the other <a href="/wiki/Way_of_the_Gods_according_to_the_Confucian_Tradition" title="Way of the Gods according to the Confucian Tradition">Luanism</a>, a cluster of churches which focus on social morality through refined Confucian ritual to worship the gods, as the "Way of Later Heaven" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">後天道</span> <i>Hòutiāndào</i>), that is the cosmological state of created things.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClart199712–13_&amp;_''passim''_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClart199712–13_&amp;_''passim''-163">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>These movements were banned in the early <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%931949)" title="Republic of China (1912–1949)">Republican China</a> and later Communist China. Many of them still remain illegal, underground or unrecognised in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, while others—specifically the De teaching, Tiandi teachings, Xuanyuan teaching, Weixinism and Yiguandao—have developed cooperation with mainland China's academic and non-governmental organisations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RCTC2014_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-RCTC2014-6">[6]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sanyi_teaching" title="Sanyi teaching">Sanyi teaching</a> is an organised folk religion founded in the 16th century, present in the <a href="/wiki/Putian" title="Putian">Putian</a> region (<a href="/wiki/Putian_people" title="Putian people">Xinghua</a>) of <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> where it is legally recognised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RCTC2014_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-RCTC2014-6">[6]</a></sup> Some of these sects began to register as branches of the state Taoist Association since the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goossaert,_Palmer._2011._p._347_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goossaert,_Palmer._2011._p._347-164">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>A further distinctive type of sects of the folk religion, that are possibly the same as the positive "secret sects", are the martial sects. They combine two aspects: the <i>wénchǎng</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">文場</span> "cultural field"), that is the doctrinal aspect characterised by elaborate cosmologies, theologies, initiatory and ritual patterns, and that is usually kept secretive; and the <i>wǔchǎng</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">武場</span> "martial field"), that is the body cultivation practice and that is usually the "public face" of the sect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ambrosi2013_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrosi2013-165">[156]</a></sup> They were outlawed by Ming imperial edicts that continued to be enforced until the fall of the Qing dynasty in the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ambrosi2013_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrosi2013-165">[156]</a></sup> An example of martial sect is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meihuaism" title="Meihuaism">Meihuaism</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">梅花教</span> <i>Méihuājiào</i>, "Plum Flowers"), that has become very popular throughout northern China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ambrosi2013_165-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrosi2013-165">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[157]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, virtually all of the "redemptive societies" operate freely since the late 1980s.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tiandi_teachings">Tiandi teachings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Tiandi teachings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Tiandi teachings are a religion that encompasses two branches, the Holy Church of the Heavenly Virtue (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">天德聖教</span> <i>Tiāndé shèngjiào</i>) and the Church of the Heavenly Deity (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">天帝教</span> <i>Tiāndìjiào</i>), both emerged from the teachings of Xiao Changming and Li Yujie, disseminated in the early 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vermander_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vermander-167">[158]</a></sup> The latter is actually an outgrowth of the former established in the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vermander_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vermander-167">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>The religions focus on the worship of <i>Tiandi</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">天帝</span>), the "Heavenly Deity" or "Heavenly Emperor",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vermander_167-2"><a href="#cite_note-Vermander-167">[158]</a></sup> on health through the proper cultivation of <a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">qi</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vermander_167-3"><a href="#cite_note-Vermander-167">[158]</a></sup> and teach a style of qigong named <i>Tianren qigong</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[159]</a></sup> According to scholars, Tiandi teachings derive from the <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoist</a> tradition of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Hua" title="Mount Hua">Huashan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[160]</a></sup> where Li Yujie studied for eight years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer201127_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer201127-170">[161]</a></sup> The Church of the Heavenly Deity is very active both in Taiwan and mainland China, where it has high-level links.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vermander_167-4"><a href="#cite_note-Vermander-167">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Weixinism">Weixinism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Weixinism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Weixinism" title="Weixinism">Weixinism</a></div>
<p>Weixinism (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">唯心聖教</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Wéixīn shèngjiào</span></i>; <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></small> 'Holy Religion of the Only Heart' or simply <span lang="zh">唯心教</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Wéixīnjiào</span></i>) is a religion primarily focused on the "orthodox lineages of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yijing" title="Yijing">Yijing</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Feng_shui" title="Feng shui">feng shui</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[162]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Schools_of_Thought" title="Hundred Schools of Thought">Hundred Schools of Thought</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldpeace_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-worldpeace-172">[163]</a></sup> and worship of the "three great ancestors" (<a href="/wiki/Yellow_Emperor" title="Yellow Emperor">Huangdi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yan_Emperor" title="Yan Emperor">Yandi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chi_You" title="Chi You">Chiyou</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[164]</a></sup> The movement promotes the restoration of the authentic roots of the Chinese civilization and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_unification" title="Chinese unification">Chinese unification</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldpeace_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-worldpeace-172">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Weixinist Church, whose headquarters are in Taiwan, is also active in <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">Mainland China</a> in the key birthplaces of the Chinese culture. It has links with the government of <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan</a> where it has established the "City of Eight Trigrams" templar complex on Yunmeng Mountain (of the <a href="/wiki/Yan_Mountains" title="Yan Mountains">Yan Mountains</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[165]</a></sup> and it has also built temples in <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">Hebei</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[166]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_and_ethnic_variations">Geographic and ethnic variations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Geographic and ethnic variations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="North_and_south_divides">North and south divides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: North and south divides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baoshengdadi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Baoshengdadi.jpg/220px-Baoshengdadi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Baoshengdadi.jpg/330px-Baoshengdadi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Baoshengdadi.jpg/440px-Baoshengdadi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baoshengdadi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Altar to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baoshengdadi" title="Baoshengdadi">Baoshengdadi</a>, whose cult is mostly <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujianese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwanese</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Recent scholarly works have found basic differences between north and south folk religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoossaertNCR_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-GoossaertNCR-176">[167]</a></sup> Folk religion of southern and southeastern provinces is focused on the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_kin" title="Chinese kin">lineages</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_lineage_associations" title="Chinese lineage associations">their churches</a> (<i>zōngzú xiéhuì</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">宗族協會</span>) focusing on ancestral gods, while the folk religion of central-northern China (<a href="/wiki/North_China_Plain" title="North China Plain">North China Plain</a>) hinges on the communal worship of <a href="/wiki/Tutelary_deity" title="Tutelary deity">tutelary deities</a> of creation and nature as identity symbols by villages populated by families of different surnames.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overmyer,_2009._pp._12-13_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-Overmyer,_2009._pp._12-13-133">[126]</a></sup> They are structured into "communities of the god(s)" (<i>shénshè</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">神社</span>, or <i>huì</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">會</span>, "association"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvermyer2009xii_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvermyer2009xii-127">[120]</a></sup> which organise temple ceremonies (<i><a href="/wiki/Miaohui" title="Miaohui">miaohui</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">廟會</span>), involving processions and pilgrimages,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[168]</a></sup> and led by indigenous ritual masters (<i>fashi</i>) who are often hereditary and linked to secular authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[note 10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Northern and southern folk religions also have a different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantheon_(gods)" title="Pantheon (gods)">pantheon</a>, of which the northern one is composed of more ancient gods of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_mythology" title="Chinese mythology">Chinese mythology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[169]</a></sup> Furthermore, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_salvationist_religions" title="Chinese salvationist religions">folk religious sects</a> have historically been more successful in the central plains and in the northeastern provinces than in southern China, and central-northern folk religion shares characteristics of some of the sects, such as the heavy importance of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_mother_goddess_worship" title="Chinese mother goddess worship">mother goddess worship</a> and shamanism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[170]</a></sup> as well as their scriptural transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoossaertNCR_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-GoossaertNCR-176">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 92">: 92 </span></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confucian_churches" title="Confucian churches">Confucian churches</a> as well have historically found much resonance among the population of the northeast; in the 1930s the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_Church_of_the_Way_and_its_Virtue" title="Universal Church of the Way and its Virtue">Universal Church of the Way and its Virtue</a> alone aggregated at least 25% of the population of the state of <a href="/wiki/Manchukuo" title="Manchukuo">Manchuria</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwnby2008_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwnby2008-181">[171]</a></sup> and contemporary <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">Shandong</a> has been analysed as an area of rapid growth of folk Confucian groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayette2016_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayette2016-182">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>Along the southeastern coast, ritual functions of the folk religion are reportedly dominated by Taoism, both in registered and unregistered forms (<a href="/wiki/Zhengyi_Dao" title="Zhengyi Dao">Zhengyi Taoism</a> and unrecognised <i>fashi</i> orders), which since the 1990s has developed quickly in the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chan,_2005._p._93_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chan,_2005._p._93-183">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overmyer-2009-southeast-Daoism_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Overmyer-2009-southeast-Daoism-184">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>Goossaert talks of this distinction, although recognising it as an oversimplification, of a "Taoist south" and a "village-religion/Confucian centre-north",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoossaertNCR_176-2"><a href="#cite_note-GoossaertNCR-176">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 47">: 47 </span></sup> with the northern context also characterised by important orders of "folk Taoist" ritual masters, one of which are the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">陰陽生</span> <i>yīnyángshēng</i> ("sages of yin and yang"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2011_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2011-185">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoossaertNCR_176-3"><a href="#cite_note-GoossaertNCR-176">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 86">: 86 </span></sup> and sectarian traditions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoossaertNCR_176-4"><a href="#cite_note-GoossaertNCR-176">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 92">: 92 </span></sup> and also by a low influence of Buddhism and official Taoism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoossaertNCR_176-5"><a href="#cite_note-GoossaertNCR-176">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 90">: 90 </span></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Northeast_China_folk_religion" title="Northeast China folk religion">folk religion of northeast China</a> has unique characteristics deriving from the interaction of Han religion with <a href="/wiki/Tungusic_peoples" title="Tungusic peoples">Tungus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manchu_shamanism" title="Manchu shamanism">Manchu shamanisms</a>; these include <i>chūmǎxiān</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">出馬仙</span> "riding for the immortals") shamanism, the worship of foxes and other <a href="/wiki/Animal_worship" title="Animal worship">zoomorphic deities</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Huxian" title="Huxian">Fox Gods</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">狐神</span> <i>Húshén</i>)—Great Lord of the Three Foxes (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">胡三太爺</span> <i>Húsān Tàiyé</i>) and the Great Lady of the Three Foxes (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">胡三太奶</span> <i>Húsān Tàinǎi</i>)—at the head of pantheons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deng-chumaxian_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deng-chumaxian-186">[176]</a></sup> Otherwise, in the <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Inner_Mongolia" title="Religion in Inner Mongolia">religious context of Inner Mongolia</a> there has been a significant integration of Han Chinese into the traditional folk religion of the region.
</p><p>In recent years<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (March 2021)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> there has also been an assimilation of deities from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tibetan_folk_religion" title="Tibetan folk religion">Tibetan folk religion</a>, especially wealth gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[177]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Tibet_Autonomous_Region" title="Tibet Autonomous Region">Tibet</a>, across broader <a href="/wiki/Western_China" title="Western China">western China</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a>, there has been a growth of the cult of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gesar" title="Gesar">Gesar</a> with the explicit support of the Chinese government, a cross-ethnic Han-Tibetan, Mongol and Manchu deity (the Han identify him as an aspect of the god of war analogically with <a href="/wiki/Guan_Yu" title="Guan Yu">Guandi</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Culture_hero" title="Culture hero">culture hero</a> whose mythology is embodied as a culturally important <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epic_poem" title="Epic poem">epic poem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[178]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id=".22Taoised.22_indigenous_religions_of_ethnic_minorities"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"Taoised"_indigenous_religions_of_ethnic_minorities'>"Taoised" indigenous religions of ethnic minorities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title='Edit section: "Taoised" indigenous religions of ethnic minorities'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benzhu_Sanxing,_Dali,_Yunnan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1232" data-file-width="2104" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Benzhu_Sanxing%2C_Dali%2C_Yunnan.jpg/220px-Benzhu_Sanxing%2C_Dali%2C_Yunnan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Benzhu_Sanxing%2C_Dali%2C_Yunnan.jpg/330px-Benzhu_Sanxing%2C_Dali%2C_Yunnan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Benzhu_Sanxing%2C_Dali%2C_Yunnan.jpg/440px-Benzhu_Sanxing%2C_Dali%2C_Yunnan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Benzhu_Sanxing,_Dali,_Yunnan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The pan-Chinese <a href="/wiki/Sanxing_(deities)" title="Sanxing (deities)">Sanxing</a> (Three Star Gods) represented in <a href="/wiki/Bai_people" title="Bai people">Bai</a> iconographic style at a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benzhu" title="Benzhu">Benzhu</a> temple on Jinsuo Island, in <a href="/wiki/Dali_Bai_Autonomous_Prefecture" title="Dali Bai Autonomous Prefecture">Dali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Chinese religion has both influenced, and in turn has been influenced by, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_religions" title="Indigenous religions">indigenous religions</a> of ethnic groups that the Han Chinese have encountered along their ethnogenetic history. Seiwert (1987) finds evidence of pre-Chinese religions in the folk religion of certain southeastern provinces such as Fujian and Taiwan, especially in the local <i>wu</i> and lineages of ordained ritual masters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seiwert1987_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seiwert1987-189">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 44">: 44 </span></sup>
</p><p>A process of <a href="/wiki/Sinicization" title="Sinicization">sinicization</a>, or more appropriately a "Taoisation", is also the more recent experience of the indigenous religions of some distinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic_minorities_of_China" title="Ethnic minorities of China">ethnic minorities of China</a>, especially southwestern people. Chinese Taoists gradually penetrate within the indigenous religions of such peoples, in some cases working side by side with indigenous priests, in other cases taking over the latter's function and integrating them by requiring their ordination as Taoists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seiwert1987_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-Seiwert1987-189">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 45">: 45 </span></sup> Usually, indigenous ritual practices remain unaffected and are adopted into Taoist liturgy, while indigenous gods are identified with Chinese gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seiwert1987_189-2"><a href="#cite_note-Seiwert1987-189">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 47">: 47 </span></sup> Seiwert discusses this phenomenon of "merger into Chinese folk religion" not as a mere elimination of non-Chinese indigenous religions, but rather as a cultural re-orientation. Local priests of southwestern ethnic minorities often acquire prestige by identifying themselves as Taoists and adopting Taoist holy texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seiwert1987_189-3"><a href="#cite_note-Seiwert1987-189">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 47">: 47 </span></sup>
</p><p>Mou (2012) writes that "Taoism has formed an indissoluble bond" with indigenous religions of southwestern ethnic minorities, especially the Tujia, <a href="/wiki/Yi_people" title="Yi people">Yi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yao_people" title="Yao people">Yao</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMou201257_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMou201257-190">[180]</a></sup> Seiwert mentions the <a href="/wiki/Miao_people" title="Miao people">Miao</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seiwert1987_189-4"><a href="#cite_note-Seiwert1987-189">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 45">: 45 </span></sup> "Daogongism" is Taoism among the <a href="/wiki/Zhuang_people" title="Zhuang people">Zhuang</a>, directed by the <i>dàogōng</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">道公</span> "lords of the Tao") and it forms an established important aspect of the broader <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhuang_folk_religion" title="Zhuang folk religion">Zhuang folk religion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the other hand, it is also true that in more recent years there has been a general revival of indigenous lineages of ritual masters without identification of these as Taoists and support from the state Chinese Taoist Church. An example is the revival of lineages of <i>bimo</i> ("scripture sages") priests among the Yi peoples. <a href="/wiki/Bimoism" title="Bimoism">Bimoism</a> has a tradition of theological literature and though clergy ordination, and this is among the reasons why it is taken in high consideration by the Chinese government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[182]</a></sup> Bamo Ayi (2001) attests that "since the early 1980s ... minority policy turned away from promoting assimilation of Han ways".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bamo_Ayi_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bamo_Ayi-193">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 118">: 118 </span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Features">Features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox" style="width:16em"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2"><small>"Chief Star pointing the Dipper" <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">魁星點斗</span> <i>Kuíxīng diǎn Dòu</i></small></th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-image" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kui_Xing_pointing_the_Big_Dipper.svg"><img alt="Kui Xing pointing the Big Dipper.svg" data-file-height="514" data-file-width="216" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Kui_Xing_pointing_the_Big_Dipper.svg/90px-Kui_Xing_pointing_the_Big_Dipper.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Kui_Xing_pointing_the_Big_Dipper.svg/135px-Kui_Xing_pointing_the_Big_Dipper.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Kui_Xing_pointing_the_Big_Dipper.svg/180px-Kui_Xing_pointing_the_Big_Dipper.svg.png 2x" width="90"/></a></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-below" colspan="2" style="text-align:left"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kuixing" title="Kuixing">Kuixing</a> ("Chief Star"), the god of exams, composed of the characters describing the four <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucian</a> virtues (<i>Sìde</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">四德</span>), standing on the head of the <i>ao</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">鰲</span>) turtle (an expression for coming first in the examinations), and pointing at the <a href="/wiki/Big_Dipper" title="Big Dipper">Big Dipper</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">斗</span>)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[note 11]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theory_of_hierarchy_and_divinity">Theory of hierarchy and divinity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Theory of hierarchy and divinity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_gods_and_immortals" title="Chinese gods and immortals">Chinese gods and immortals</a></div>
<p>Chinese religions are <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheistic</a>, meaning that many deities are worshipped as part of what has been defined as <i>yǔzhòu shénlùn</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">宇宙神論</span>), translated as "<a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">cosmotheism</a>", a worldview in which divinity is inherent to the world itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._71_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._71-84">[81]</a></sup> The gods (<i><a href="/wiki/Shen_(Chinese_religion)" title="Shen (Chinese religion)">shen</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">神</span>; "growth", "beings that give birth"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._63_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._63-196">[185]</a></sup>) are interwoven energies or principles that generate phenomena which reveal or reproduce the way of Heaven, that is to say the order (<i><a href="/wiki/Li_(Confucianism)" title="Li (Confucianism)">li</a></i>) of the Greatnine(<i><a href="/wiki/Tian" title="Tian">Tian</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-names_of_Heaven_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-names_of_Heaven-76">[note 2]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Chinese tradition, there is not a clear distinction between the gods and their physical body or bodies (from stars to trees and animals);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010158_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010158-197">[186]</a></sup> the qualitative difference between the two seems not to have ever been emphasised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010158_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010158-197">[186]</a></sup> Rather, the disparity is said to be more quantitative than qualitative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010158_197-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010158-197">[186]</a></sup> In doctrinal terms, the Chinese view of gods is related to the understanding of <i><a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">qi</a></i>, the life force,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010158_197-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010158-197">[186]</a></sup> as the gods and their phenomenal productions are manifestations of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010158_197-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010158-197">[186]</a></sup> In this way, all natural bodies are believed to be able to attain supernatural attributes by acting according to the universal oneness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010158_197-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010158-197">[186]</a></sup> Meanwhile, acting wickedly (that is to say against the Tian and its order) brings to disgrace and disaster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010165_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010165-198">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>In folk religions, gods (<i>shen</i>) and immortals (<i><a href="/wiki/Xian_(Taoism)" title="Xian (Taoism)">xian</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">仙</span>) are not specifically distinguished from each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010161_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010161-199">[188]</a></sup> Gods can incarnate in human form and human beings can reach immortality, which means to attain higher spirituality, since all the spiritual principles (gods) are begotten of the primordial <i>qi</i> before any physical manifestation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010159_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010159-109">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Doctrine_of_the_Mean" title="Doctrine of the Mean">Doctrine of the Mean</a></i>, one of the Confucian four books, the <i><a href="/wiki/Zhenren" title="Zhenren">zhenren</a></i> (wise) is the man who has achieved a spiritual status developing his true sincere nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010162_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010162-200">[189]</a></sup> This status, in turn, enables him to fully develop the true nature of others and of all things.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010162_200-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010162-200">[189]</a></sup> The sage is able to "assist the transforming and nourishing process of Heaven and Earth", forming a trinity (三才 <i>Sāncái</i>, the "Three Powers") with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010162_200-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010162-200">[189]</a></sup> In other words, in the Chinese tradition humans are or can be the medium between Heaven and Earth, and have the role of completing what had been initiated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sancai_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sancai-112">[note 7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Taoist schools in particular espouse an explicit spiritual pathway which pushes the earthly beings to the edge of eternity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010164_111-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010164-111">[105]</a></sup> Since the human body is a microcosm, enlivened by the universal order of yin and yang like the whole cosmos, the means of immortality can be found within oneself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010164_111-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010164-111">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among those worshipped as immortal heroes (<i>xian</i>, exalted beings) are historical individuals distinguished for their worth or bravery, those who taught crafts to others and formed societies establishing the order of Heaven, ancestors or progenitors (<i>zu</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">祖</span>), and the creators of a spiritual tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010162,_165_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010162,_165-107">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[190]</a></sup> The concept of "human divinity" is not self-contradictory, as there is no unbridgeable gap between the two realms; rather, the divine and the human are mutually contained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010162_200-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010162-200">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p>In comparison with gods of an environmental nature, who tend to remain stable throughout human experience and history, individual human deities change in time. Some endure for centuries, while others remain localised cults, or vanish after a short time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010158_197-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010158-197">[186]</a></sup> Immortal beings are conceived as "constellations of <a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">qi</a>", which is so vibrant in certain historical individuals that, upon the person's death, this <i>qi</i> nexus does not dissipate but persists, and is reinforced by living people's worship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnett_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barnett-202">[191]</a></sup> The energetic power of a god is thought to reverberate on the worshipers influencing their fortune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnett_202-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barnett-202">[191]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deities_and_immortals">Deities and immortals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Deities and immortals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yellow_God_incarnation_theology" title="Yellow God incarnation theology">Yellow God incarnation theology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Doumu_altar_and_statue_at_the_Doumugong_of_Butterworth,_Penang.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1424" data-file-width="1836" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Doumu_altar_and_statue_at_the_Doumugong_of_Butterworth%2C_Penang.jpg/220px-Doumu_altar_and_statue_at_the_Doumugong_of_Butterworth%2C_Penang.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Doumu_altar_and_statue_at_the_Doumugong_of_Butterworth%2C_Penang.jpg/330px-Doumu_altar_and_statue_at_the_Doumugong_of_Butterworth%2C_Penang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Doumu_altar_and_statue_at_the_Doumugong_of_Butterworth%2C_Penang.jpg/440px-Doumu_altar_and_statue_at_the_Doumugong_of_Butterworth%2C_Penang.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Doumu_altar_and_statue_at_the_Doumugong_of_Butterworth,_Penang.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Main altar and statue of <a href="/wiki/Doumu" title="Doumu">Doumu</a> inside the Temple of Doumu in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Butterworth,_Penang" title="Butterworth, Penang">Butterworth, Penang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E7%82%8E%E9%BB%84%E4%BA%8C%E5%B8%9D%E5%B7%A8%E5%9E%8B%E5%A1%91%E5%83%8F%E6%AD%A3%E9%9D%A2%E8%A7%86%E8%A7%92.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2904" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/%E7%82%8E%E9%BB%84%E4%BA%8C%E5%B8%9D%E5%B7%A8%E5%9E%8B%E5%A1%91%E5%83%8F%E6%AD%A3%E9%9D%A2%E8%A7%86%E8%A7%92.JPG/220px-%E7%82%8E%E9%BB%84%E4%BA%8C%E5%B8%9D%E5%B7%A8%E5%9E%8B%E5%A1%91%E5%83%8F%E6%AD%A3%E9%9D%A2%E8%A7%86%E8%A7%92.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/%E7%82%8E%E9%BB%84%E4%BA%8C%E5%B8%9D%E5%B7%A8%E5%9E%8B%E5%A1%91%E5%83%8F%E6%AD%A3%E9%9D%A2%E8%A7%86%E8%A7%92.JPG/330px-%E7%82%8E%E9%BB%84%E4%BA%8C%E5%B8%9D%E5%B7%A8%E5%9E%8B%E5%A1%91%E5%83%8F%E6%AD%A3%E9%9D%A2%E8%A7%86%E8%A7%92.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/%E7%82%8E%E9%BB%84%E4%BA%8C%E5%B8%9D%E5%B7%A8%E5%9E%8B%E5%A1%91%E5%83%8F%E6%AD%A3%E9%9D%A2%E8%A7%86%E8%A7%92.JPG/440px-%E7%82%8E%E9%BB%84%E4%BA%8C%E5%B8%9D%E5%B7%A8%E5%9E%8B%E5%A1%91%E5%83%8F%E6%AD%A3%E9%9D%A2%E8%A7%86%E8%A7%92.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E7%82%8E%E9%BB%84%E4%BA%8C%E5%B8%9D%E5%B7%A8%E5%9E%8B%E5%A1%91%E5%83%8F%E6%AD%A3%E9%9D%A2%E8%A7%86%E8%A7%92.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue and ceremonial complex of the Yellow and Red Gods, from whom the <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> are <a href="/wiki/Yan_Huang_Zisun" title="Yan Huang Zisun">said to be the descendants</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Zhengzhou" title="Zhengzhou">Zhengzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Gods and immortals (collectively <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">神仙</span> <i>shénxiān</i>) in the Chinese cultural tradition reflect a hierarchical, <a href="/wiki/Multiperspectivity" title="Multiperspectivity">multiperspective</a> experience of divinity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._63_196-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._63-196">[185]</a></sup> In Chinese language there is a terminological distinction between <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">神</span> <i>shén</i>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">帝</span> <i>dì</i> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">仙</span> <i>xiān</i>. Although the usage of the former two is sometimes blurred, it corresponds to the distinction in Western cultures between "god" and "deity", Latin <i><a href="/wiki/Genius_(mythology)" title="Genius (mythology)">genius</a></i> (meaning a generative principle, "spirit") and <i><a href="/wiki/Deus" title="Deus">deus</a></i> or <i>divus</i>; <i>dì</i>, sometimes translated as "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/thearch" title="wiktionary:thearch">thearch</a>", implies a manifested or incarnate "godly" power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[note 12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMedhurst1847[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidkw1gAAAAcAAJpgPA260vonepageqffalse_260]_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMedhurst1847[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidkw1gAAAAcAAJpgPA260vonepageqffalse_260]-205">[193]</a></sup> It is etymologically and figuratively analogous to the concept of <i>di</i> as the base of a fruit, which falls and produces other fruits. This analogy is attested in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shuowen_jiezi" title="Shuowen jiezi">Shuowen jiezi</a></i> explaining "deity" as "what faces the base of a melon fruit".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhao201251_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhao201251-206">[194]</a></sup> The latter term <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">仙</span> <i>xiān</i> unambiguously means a man who has reached immortality, similarly to the Western idea of "<a href="/wiki/Hero" title="Hero">hero</a>".
</p><p>Many classical books have lists and hierarchies of gods and immortals, among which the "Completed Record of Deities and Immortals" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">神仙通鑒</span> <i>Shénxiān tōngjiàn</i>) of the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010159_109-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010159-109">[103]</a></sup> and the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biographies_of_Deities_and_Immortals" title="Biographies of Deities and Immortals">Biographies of Deities and Immortals</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">神仙傳</span> <i>Shénxiān zhuán</i>) by <a href="/wiki/Ge_Hong" title="Ge Hong">Ge Hong</a> (284–343).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010161_199-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010161-199">[188]</a></sup> There's also the older <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liexian_zhuan" title="Liexian zhuan">Liexian zhuan</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">列仙傳</span> "Collected Biographies of Immortals").
</p><p>There are the great cosmic gods representing the first principle in its unmanifested state or its creative order—<a href="/wiki/Jade_Emperor" title="Jade Emperor">Yudi</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">玉帝</span> "Jade Deity")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-king-emperor-shaman-axis_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-king-emperor-shaman-axis-83">[note 3]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Doumu" title="Doumu">Doumu</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">斗母</span> "Mother of the Meaning" or "Great Chariot"), <a href="/wiki/Pangu" title="Pangu">Pangu</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">盤古</span>, the <a href="/wiki/Macranthropy" title="Macranthropy">macranthropic</a> metaphor of the cosmos), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xiwangmu" title="Xiwangmu">Xiwangmu</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">西王母</span> "Queen Mother of the West") and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dongwanggong" title="Dongwanggong">Dongwanggong</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">東王公</span> "King Duke of the East") who personificate respectively the yin and the yang,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2005[httpsbooksgooglecombookshlenid9wi-ZDdmaqECdqisbn3A1845190866qXiwangmuvsnippetqXiwangmuffalse_206–207]_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2005[httpsbooksgooglecombookshlenid9wi-ZDdmaqECdqisbn3A1845190866qXiwangmuvsnippetqXiwangmuffalse_206–207]-207">[195]</a></sup> as well as the dimensional Three Patrons and the <a href="/wiki/Wufang_Shangdi" title="Wufang Shangdi">Five Deities</a>; then there are the sky and weather gods, the scenery gods,  the vegetal and animal gods, and gods of human virtues and crafts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._71_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._71-84">[81]</a></sup> These are interpreted in different ways in Taoism and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_salvationist_religions" title="Chinese salvationist religions">folk sects</a>, the former conferring them long <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kataphatic" title="Kataphatic">kataphatic</a> names.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._71_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lu,_Gong._2014._p._71-84">[81]</a></sup> Below the great deities, there is the unquantifiable number of gods of nature, as every phenomena have or are gods.
</p><p>The Three Patrons (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">三皇</span> <i>Sānhuáng</i>)—<a href="/wiki/Fuxi" title="Fuxi">Fuxi</a>, <a href="/wiki/N%C3%BCwa" title="Nüwa">Nüwa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shennong" title="Shennong">Shennong</a>—are the "vertical" manifestation of the primordial God corresponding to the Three Realms (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">三界</span> <i>Sānjiè</i>), representing the yin and yang and the medium between them, that is the human being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Five Deities (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">五帝</span> <i>Wǔdì</i>) or "Five Forms of the Highest Deity" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">五方上帝</span> <i>Wǔfāng Shàngdì</i>)—the Yellow, Green or Blue, <a href="/wiki/Heidi_(god)" title="Heidi (god)">Black</a>, Red and White Deities<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMedhurst1847[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidkw1gAAAAcAAJpgPA260vonepageqffalse_260]_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMedhurst1847[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidkw1gAAAAcAAJpgPA260vonepageqffalse_260]-205">[193]</a></sup>—are the five "horizontal" manifestations of the primordial God and according with the Three Realms they have a celestial, a terrestrial and a chthonic form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[note 13]</a></sup> They correspond to the <a href="/wiki/Wuxing_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Wuxing (Chinese philosophy)">five phases of creation</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_Symbols_(China)" title="Four Symbols (China)">five constellations rotating around the celestial pole</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sacred_Mountains_of_China" title="Sacred Mountains of China">five sacred mountains</a> and the five directions of space (the four <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_direction" title="Cardinal direction">cardinal directions</a> and the centre), and the five Dragon Gods (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">龍神</span> <i>Lóngshén</i>) which represent their mounts, that is to say the chthonic forces they preside over.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleEichman2000250_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleEichman2000250-211">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESunKistemaker1997[httpsbooksgooglecombookshlenid87lvBoFi8A0CqHuangdivsnippetqHuangdiffalse_120–123]_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESunKistemaker1997[httpsbooksgooglecombookshlenid87lvBoFi8A0CqHuangdivsnippetqHuangdiffalse_120–123]-212">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yellow_God" title="Yellow God">Yellow God</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">黃神</span> <i>Huángshén</i>) or "Yellow God of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Dipper" title="Big Dipper">Northern Dipper</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">黃神北斗</span> <i>Huángshén Běidǒu</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[note 14]</a></sup>) is of peculiar importance, as he is a form of the universal God (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_theology" title="Chinese theology">Tian or Shangdi</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELagerweyKalinowski20081080_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELagerweyKalinowski20081080-214">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPregadio2013[httpsbooksgooglecombookshlenidR3Sp6TfzhpICqHuangdivsnippetqHuangdiffalse_504–505],_vol._2_A-L_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPregadio2013[httpsbooksgooglecombookshlenidR3Sp6TfzhpICqHuangdivsnippetqHuangdiffalse_504–505],_vol._2_A-L-215">[201]</a></sup> symbolising the <i><a href="/wiki/Axis_mundi" title="Axis mundi">axis mundi</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kunlun_Mountain_(mythology)" title="Kunlun Mountain (mythology)">Kunlun</a>), or the intersection between the Three Patrons and the Five Deities, that is the center of the cosmos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2005[httpsbooksgooglecombookshlenid9wi-ZDdmaqECdqisbn3A1845190866qHuangdivsnippetqHuangdiffalse_200–201]_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2005[httpsbooksgooglecombookshlenid9wi-ZDdmaqECdqisbn3A1845190866qHuangdivsnippetqHuangdiffalse_200–201]-216">[202]</a></sup> He is therefore described in the <i><a href="/wiki/Shizi_(book)" title="Shizi (book)">Shizi</a></i> as the "Yellow Emperor with Four Faces" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">黃帝四面</span> <i>Huángdì Sìmiàn</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESunKistemaker1997120_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESunKistemaker1997120-217">[203]</a></sup> His human incarnation, the "Yellow Emperor (or Deity) of the Mysterious Origin" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">軒轅黃帝</span> <i>Xuānyuán Huángdì</i>), is said to be the creator of the <i><a href="/wiki/Huaxia" title="Huaxia">Huaxia</a></i> civility, of marriage and morality, language and lineage, and <a href="/wiki/Yan_Huang_Zisun" title="Yan Huang Zisun">patriarch of all the Chinese</a> together with the Red Deity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChamberlain2009222_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChamberlain2009222-218">[204]</a></sup> Xuanyuan was the fruit of virginal birth, as his mother Fubao conceived him as she was aroused, while walking in the country, by a lightning from the Big Dipper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[205]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mother_goddess_worship">Mother goddess worship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Mother goddess worship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E7%A2%A7%E9%9C%9E%E7%A5%A0.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/%E7%A2%A7%E9%9C%9E%E7%A5%A0.jpg/220px-%E7%A2%A7%E9%9C%9E%E7%A5%A0.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/%E7%A2%A7%E9%9C%9E%E7%A5%A0.jpg/330px-%E7%A2%A7%E9%9C%9E%E7%A5%A0.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/%E7%A2%A7%E9%9C%9E%E7%A5%A0.jpg/440px-%E7%A2%A7%E9%9C%9E%E7%A5%A0.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E7%A2%A7%E9%9C%9E%E7%A5%A0.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shrine of Bixia at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Tai" title="Mount Tai">Mount Tai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">Shandong</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The worship of <a href="/wiki/Mother_goddess" title="Mother goddess">mother goddesses</a> for the cultivation of offspring is present all over China, but predominantly in northern provinces. There are nine main goddesses, and all of them tend to be considered as manifestations or attendant forces of a singular goddess identified variously as Bixia (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">碧霞</span> "Blue Dawn"), the daughter or female consort of the Green God of Mount Tai, or <a href="/wiki/Houtu" title="Houtu">Houtu</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">后土</span> the "Queen of the Earth").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones,_2013._pp._166-167_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones,_2013._pp._166-167-220">[206]</a></sup> Bixia herself is identified by Taoists as the more ancient goddess <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xiwangmu" title="Xiwangmu">Xiwangmu</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[207]</a></sup> Goddesses are commonly entitled <i>mǔ</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">母</span> "mother"), <i>lǎomǔ</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">老母</span> "old mother"), <i>shèngmǔ</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">聖母</span> "holy mother"), <i>niángniáng</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">娘娘</span> "lady"), <i>nǎinai</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">奶奶</span> "granny").
</p><p>Altars of goddess worship are usually arranged with Bixia at the center and two goddesses at her sides, most frequently the Lady of Eyesight and the Lady of Offspring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wicks_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wicks-222">[208]</a></sup> A different figure but with the same astral connections as Bixia is the Qixing Niangniang (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">七星娘娘</span> "Goddess of the Seven Stars"). There is also the cluster of the Holy Mothers of the Three Skies (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">三霄聖母</span> <i>Sanxiao Shengmu</i>; or "Ladies of the Three Skies", <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">三霄娘娘</span> <i>Sanxiao Niangniang</i>), composed of <i>Yunxiao Guniang</i>, <i>Qiongxiao Guniang</i> and <i>Bixiao Guniang</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvermyer2009135_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvermyer2009135-223">[209]</a></sup> In southeastern provinces the cult of <a href="/wiki/Chen_Jinggu" title="Chen Jinggu">Chen Jinggu</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">陳靖姑</span>) is identified by some scholars as an emanation of the northern cult of Bixia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[210]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are other local goddesses with motherly features, including the northern Canmu (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">蠶母</span> "Silkworm Mother") and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazu_(goddess)" title="Mazu (goddess)">Mazu</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">媽祖</span> "Ancestral Mother"), popular in provinces along the eastern coast and in Taiwan. The title of "Queen of Heaven" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">天后</span> <i>Tiānhòu</i>) is most frequently attributed to Mazu and Doumu (the cosmic goddess).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Worship_and_modalities_of_religious_practice">Worship and modalities of religious practice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Worship and modalities of religious practice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Procession_with_xingshen_(traveling_image_of_the_god)_in_central_Taiwan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="511" data-file-width="798" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Procession_with_xingshen_%28traveling_image_of_the_god%29_in_central_Taiwan.jpg/220px-Procession_with_xingshen_%28traveling_image_of_the_god%29_in_central_Taiwan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Procession_with_xingshen_%28traveling_image_of_the_god%29_in_central_Taiwan.jpg/330px-Procession_with_xingshen_%28traveling_image_of_the_god%29_in_central_Taiwan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Procession_with_xingshen_%28traveling_image_of_the_god%29_in_central_Taiwan.jpg/440px-Procession_with_xingshen_%28traveling_image_of_the_god%29_in_central_Taiwan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Procession_with_xingshen_(traveling_image_of_the_god)_in_central_Taiwan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Procession with a traveling image of a god (<i>xíngshén</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">行神</span>) in central <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_temple%27s_vows_to_the_deity.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4272" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Chinese_temple%27s_vows_to_the_deity.jpg/220px-Chinese_temple%27s_vows_to_the_deity.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Chinese_temple%27s_vows_to_the_deity.jpg/330px-Chinese_temple%27s_vows_to_the_deity.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Chinese_temple%27s_vows_to_the_deity.jpg/440px-Chinese_temple%27s_vows_to_the_deity.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_temple%27s_vows_to_the_deity.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vows to a deity at a Chinese temple in <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taoist_ceremony_at_Xiao_ancestral_temple_in_Chaoyang,_Shantou,_Guangdong_(inside)_(4).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Taoist_ceremony_at_Xiao_ancestral_temple_in_Chaoyang%2C_Shantou%2C_Guangdong_%28inside%29_%284%29.jpg/220px-Taoist_ceremony_at_Xiao_ancestral_temple_in_Chaoyang%2C_Shantou%2C_Guangdong_%28inside%29_%284%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Taoist_ceremony_at_Xiao_ancestral_temple_in_Chaoyang%2C_Shantou%2C_Guangdong_%28inside%29_%284%29.jpg/330px-Taoist_ceremony_at_Xiao_ancestral_temple_in_Chaoyang%2C_Shantou%2C_Guangdong_%28inside%29_%284%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Taoist_ceremony_at_Xiao_ancestral_temple_in_Chaoyang%2C_Shantou%2C_Guangdong_%28inside%29_%284%29.jpg/440px-Taoist_ceremony_at_Xiao_ancestral_temple_in_Chaoyang%2C_Shantou%2C_Guangdong_%28inside%29_%284%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Taoist_ceremony_at_Xiao_ancestral_temple_in_Chaoyang,_Shantou,_Guangdong_(inside)_(4).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Taoist rite for ancestor worship at the <a href="/wiki/Xiao_(surname)" title="Xiao (surname)">Xiao</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestral_temple" title="Ancestral temple">ancestral temple</a> of <a href="/wiki/Chaoyang_District,_Shantou" title="Chaoyang District, Shantou">Chaoyang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shantou" title="Shantou">Shantou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Adam Yuet Chau identifies five styles or modalities of "doing" Chinese religion:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChau2011_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChau2011-225">[211]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Discursive-scriptural: involving the composition, preaching, and recitation of texts (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_classics" title="Chinese classics">classics</a>, Taoist scriptures and morality books);</li>
<li>Personal cultivation mode, involving a long-term cultivation and transformation of oneself with the goal of becoming a <i><a href="/wiki/Xian_(Taoism)" title="Xian (Taoism)">xian</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">仙</span> (immortal), <i><a href="/wiki/Zhenren" title="Zhenren">zhenren</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">真人</span> ("true person"), or <i>shengren</i> (wise), through the practice of different "technologies of the self" (<i><a href="/wiki/Qigong" title="Qigong">qigong</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">氣功</span>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_alchemy" title="Chinese alchemy">Taoist inner and outer alchemy</a>, charitable acts for merit, memorisation and recitation of texts);</li>
<li>Liturgical: involving elaborate ritual procedures conducted by specialists of rites (Taoist rites, Confucian rites, Nuo rites, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fengshui" title="Fengshui">fengshui</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">風水</span>);</li>
<li>Immediate practical: aiming at quick efficacious (<i>ling</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">靈</span>) results through simple ritual and magical techniques (<a href="/wiki/Divination" title="Divination">divination</a>, <a href="/wiki/Talisman" title="Talisman">talismans</a>, divine medicine, consulting media and shamans);</li>
<li>Relational: emphasising the devotional relationship between men and deities and among men themselves (organising elaborate <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">sacrifices</a>, making vows, organising temple festivals, <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">pilgrimages</a>, processions, and religious communities) in "social comings and goings" (<i>laiwang</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">來往</span>) and "interconnectedness" (<i><a href="/wiki/Guanxi" title="Guanxi">guanxi</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">關係</span>).</li></ul>
<p>Generally speaking, the Chinese believe that spiritual and material well-being ensues from the harmony of humanity and gods in their participation in the same cosmic power, and also believe that by taking the right path and practice anybody is able to reach the absolute reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010173_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010173-226">[212]</a></sup> Religious practice is therefore regarded as the bridge to link the human world to the spiritual source,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010173_226-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010173-226">[212]</a></sup> maintaining the harmony of the micro and macrocosmos, protecting the individual and the world from disruption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183_125-6"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183-125">[118]</a></sup> In this sense, the Chinese view of human life is not deterministic, but one is a master of his own life and can choose to collaborate with the deities for a harmonious world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183_125-7"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183-125">[118]</a></sup>
Chinese culture being a <a href="/wiki/Holism" title="Holism">holistic</a> system, in which every aspect is a part of a whole, Chinese folk religious practice is often intermingled with political, educational and economic concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010172_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010172-227">[213]</a></sup> A gathering or event may be encompassed with all of these aspects; in general, the commitment (belief) and the process or rite (practice) together form the internal and external dimensions of Chinese religious life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010172_227-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010172-227">[213]</a></sup> In village communities, religious services are often organised and led by local people themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20139_49-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20139-49">[49]</a></sup> Leaders are usually selected among male heads of families or lineages, or village heads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen20139_49-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen20139-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>A simple form of individual practice is to show respect for the gods (<i>jing shen</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">敬神</span>) through <i><a href="/wiki/Jingxiang" title="Jingxiang">jingxiang</a></i> (incense offering), and the exchange of vows (<i>huan yuan</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">還願</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183_125-8"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._183-125">[118]</a></sup> Sacrifice can consist of incense, oil, and candles, as well as money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201310_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201310-228">[214]</a></sup> Religious devotion may also express in the form of performance troupes (<i>huahui</i>), involving many types of professionals such as stilt walkers, lion dancers, musicians, martial arts masters, <i><a href="/wiki/Yangge" title="Yangge">yangge</a></i> dancers, and story-tellers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201310_228-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201310-228">[214]</a></sup>
</p><p>Deities can also be respected through moral deeds in their name (<i>shanshi</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">善事</span>), and self-cultivation (<i>xiuxing</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">修行</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[215]</a></sup> Some forms of folk religion develop clear prescriptions for believers, such as detailed lists of meritorious and sinful deeds in the form of "morality books" (<i>shanshu</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">善書</span>) and ledgers of merit and demerit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._182_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._182-230">[216]</a></sup> Involvement in the affairs of communal or intra-village temples are perceived by believers as ways for accumulating merit (<i>gongde</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">功德</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._182_230-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._182-230">[216]</a></sup> Virtue is believed to accumulate in one's heart, which is seen as energetic centre of the human body (<i>zai jun xin zuo tian fu</i> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">在君心作福田</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[217]</a></sup> Practices of communication with the gods comprehend different forms of Chinese shamanism, such as <a href="/wiki/Wu_(shaman)" title="Wu (shaman)"><i>wu</i> shamanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tongji_(spirit_medium)" title="Tongji (spirit medium)"><i>tongji</i> mediumship</a>, or <i><a href="/wiki/Fuji_(planchette_writing)" title="Fuji (planchette writing)">fuji</a></i> practice.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sacrifices">Sacrifices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Sacrifices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E9%A9%AC%E9%99%8D%E9%BE%99_04_-_%E7%8C%AA%E8%82%89%E7%9B%98.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4096" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/%E9%A9%AC%E9%99%8D%E9%BE%99_04_-_%E7%8C%AA%E8%82%89%E7%9B%98.jpg/220px-%E9%A9%AC%E9%99%8D%E9%BE%99_04_-_%E7%8C%AA%E8%82%89%E7%9B%98.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/%E9%A9%AC%E9%99%8D%E9%BE%99_04_-_%E7%8C%AA%E8%82%89%E7%9B%98.jpg/330px-%E9%A9%AC%E9%99%8D%E9%BE%99_04_-_%E7%8C%AA%E8%82%89%E7%9B%98.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/%E9%A9%AC%E9%99%8D%E9%BE%99_04_-_%E7%8C%AA%E8%82%89%E7%9B%98.jpg/440px-%E9%A9%AC%E9%99%8D%E9%BE%99_04_-_%E7%8C%AA%E8%82%89%E7%9B%98.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E9%A9%AC%E9%99%8D%E9%BE%99_04_-_%E7%8C%AA%E8%82%89%E7%9B%98.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tray for offering sacrifices, on display in <a href="/wiki/Kaiping" title="Kaiping">Kaiping</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Classical Chinese</a> has characters for different types of <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">sacrifice</a>, probably the oldest way to communicate with divine forces, today generally encompassed by the definition <i>jìsì</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">祭祀</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010173_226-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010173-226">[212]</a></sup> However different in scale and quantity, all types of sacrifice would normally involve food, wine, meat and later incense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010176_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010176-232">[218]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sacrifices usually differ according to the kind of deity they are devoted to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010176_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010176-232">[218]</a></sup> Traditionally, cosmic and nature gods are offered uncooked (or whole) food, while ancestors are offered cooked food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010176_232-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010176-232">[218]</a></sup> Moreover, sacrifices for gods are made inside the temples that enshrine them, while sacrifices for ancestors are made outside temples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010176_232-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010176-232">[218]</a></sup> Yearly sacrifices (<i>ji</i>) are made to Confucius, the Red and Yellow Emperors, and other cultural heroes and ancestors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010176_232-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010176-232">[218]</a></sup>
</p><p>Both in past history and at the present, all sacrifices are assigned with both religious and political purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010177_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010177-233">[219]</a></sup> Some gods are considered carnivorous, for example the River God (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">河神</span> <i>Héshén</i>) and Dragon Gods, and offering to them requires animal sacrifice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._189_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zavidovskaya,_2012._p._189-234">[220]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Thanksgiving_and_redeeming">Thanksgiving and redeeming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Thanksgiving and redeeming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The aims of rituals and sacrifices may be of thanksgiving and redeeming, usually involving both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010177_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010177-233">[219]</a></sup> Various sacrifices are intended to express gratitude toward the gods in the hope that spiritual blessing and protection will continue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010177_233-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010177-233">[219]</a></sup> The <i>jiào</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">醮</span></span>), an elaborate Taoist sacrifice or "rite of universal salvation", is intended to be a cosmic community renewal, that is to say a reconciliation of a community around its spiritual centre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010178_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010178-235">[221]</a></sup> The <i>jiao</i> ritual usually starts with <i>zhai</i>, "fasting and purification", that is meant as an atonement for evil-doing, then followed by sacrificial offerings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010178_235-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010178-235">[221]</a></sup>
</p><p>This rite, of great political importance, can be intended for the whole nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010178_235-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010178-235">[221]</a></sup> In fact, as early as the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a>, emperors asked renowned Taoists to perform such rituals on their behalf or for the entire nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010178_235-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010178-235">[221]</a></sup> The modern Chinese republic has given approval for Taoists to conduct such rituals since the 1990s, with the aim of protecting the country and the nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao2010178_235-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao2010178-235">[221]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rites_of_passage">Rites of passage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_folk_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Rites of passage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
